“THE BULLETIN” BOOK EXCHANGE.
On receipt at The Bulletin Office, Sydney, of Postal Order, Postal Notes, or Stamps (of any province) equal to the price stated
(which is published price with postage added), books in the subjoined list will be forwarded to any Australasian address.
CONAN DOYLE’S NOVELS.
Conan Doyle is one of the most popular and
prolific of contemporary English story-tellers.
His vogue is hardly five years old; yet, within
those five years, he has published four substantial
novels and a multitude of short tales —in all, a
round dozen volumes, of which the sale exceeds
100,000 copies. His fertility is an index to his
literary status, for great works are not ground
out mechanically for the publishers. His popu-
larity is another such index, for great works are
rarely popular. Survey of Doyle’s performances
justifies the a 'priori suspicion that his success
is but a success of mediocrity. His writings suit
his public : they are not too good, but just good
enough. His chief virtue is his thoroughness—
the characteristic Scotch tenacity which gives the
lie to the theory that genius may be identified
with an infinite capacity for taking pains. There
is nothing of genius in Doyle ; but he has homely
talent and splendid industry. Considering its
extent, the uniformity of his work is surprising.
“ The Firm of Girdlestone” and half-a-dozen of
his short stories are all that can be ranked as
absolute failures ; and even the fotmer —a novel
of the newspaper order of architecture, skeletoned
off under heads and sub-heads, and then
laboriously filled in—seems to have interest for
readers of conventional trash-fiction.
# • *
Doyle, indeed, rarely fails in interest. He has
little originality of imagination; but give him a
foundation and he displays considerable originality
of workmanship. The keynote of his work is
conscientiousness: he chooses his incidents well,
and strains hard to po md out of each its last
atom of literary efficiency. Some of Sherlock
Holmes’ outre conclusions may seem to conflict
with this estimate; but they are none of them
imaginative leaps —all solid resuits of masculine
reasoning, built up step by s ep like a theorem
in mathematics. Doyle is essentially a short-
story writer. His novels are a mere suc-
cession of episodes through which stalk
characters without individuality or intrinsic
interest. “ The White Company.” his magnum
opus, is cons ructed with immense pains on the
best models. It has considerable interest of a
mechanical kind ; but the inspiration is as differ-
ent from that of genius as a maiden aunt from a
married angel. As in “ The Finn of Girdle-
stone,” the author has pieced together an elaborate
skeleton and laboriously covered it with flesh. It
is admirably done; but the man is not alive—is
only an image of life. The color and scenery are
very well given ; but the humor is poor, and there
is no art to conceal the art. You realise that
Doyle has carefully studied the methods by which
historical novelis's from Scott to Flaubert have
created their interest. There is one wrestling-
match, one duel on foot, one ditto on horseback,
one pitched battle, one attack on a castle (the
villeins are novel and effective), one sea-fight—
in fact, the whole box of tricks is carefully un-
packed ; and each is taken out, earnestly shown,
and put away again. It is an irritating fashion,
and damps enthusiasm for a really fine piece of
work.
# * *
Again, Doyle cannot always hide his echo. You
hear Kingsley in Hordle John’s eruption from the
monastery ; and Lytton is suggested later on.
Dumas and Cooper play parts in “Theßefugees ”
—an exciting story full of the handiwork of the
machine-god. In “ Micah Clarke,” Doyle echoes
himself; for Micah is little but but a replica of
Hordle John, with a touch of Alleyne, and he
plods wearily through the adventures laid down
for him. It is a pleasure to come suddenly upon
an infrequent twinkle like Gervas Jerome’s : “It
is a good rule to go upon, Clarke, in this earthly
pilgrimage, always to kiss the landlady. . . I
always carry a corkscrew, and I never forget to
kiss the landlady. ” “ t A Study in Scarlet, ” “ The
Sign of Four,” “ The Great Shadow,” and some
of the talcs in the “ Polestar ” volume, rank high
for interest, whatever their deficiencies as classical
fiction. It is doubtless unfair to judge work so
good in its way by the standard which one brings
to a professed masterpiece. “Raffles Haw” has
a good central idea, but only ephemeral merit;
and the recent “ Parasite ” and “Mystery of
Cloomber ” fall in the same category. In “ Round
the Red Lamp ” are found some of the author’s
best stories—with a deeper intellectual quality
than most of his earlier work, and a fine, thought-
ful fascination. Doyle, in fact, is generally worth
reading and rarely worth remembering. One
accompanies him with pleasure, and leaves him
without very much regret.

O TJRSELVES!
We wish it. to tie understood that the character of the
business we do, a d the absolutely inc usive variety of the
goods we Bill, entitle us to be called
UNIVERSAL PROVIDERS.
We are also not far out, consid' ring the low prices we
ask for everything we sell, and the consequent blessings
w« are continually showering upon the people, when we
assert, not in t nmrous tones, but with the trumpet voice
of confidence in the truth of what we say, when we dub
ourselves
PUBLIC BENEFACTORS.
There are good men in every sphere of life—honest
citizens in every community, whose deeds stamp them
for the approval of their f How-men, and single them out
from the common herd. Thus we find among the crowd
who aspire to serve their country on the benches of the
Legislative Assembly a patriot here and there w ho stands
out in hold r lief against the majority, whose sole aim
is place and pay. And in commercial life the sam 1 thing
occurs, and the same strong contrast between live few
disinterested- friends of the people, and the many s Ifish
lookers after the leg prolts they would like to make, is
plainly apparent. To the forra -r class, to the little hand
of merchants who study their customers’ interests, and
who, in self-abnegation and contentednegs, are willing to
accept small profits in reward for their services, belong
ANTHONY HORDERN’S.
They say that a good action is its own reward, but if the
good action entail hard work, much tribulation, and con-
linual strife, then surely the laborer is worthy of his hire,
at d the doer of good actions entitled to some recompense
at the hands of those who profit by what he does.
ANTHONY HORDERN’S
nevercease from their toil ; in the burden and heat of the
noonday sun lh<y work, oppressed by the cruel frost of
somewhile baffled efforts and frustrated negotiations, they
continue, knowing alw’ays that the approval of millions of
souls is theirs. For three-quarters of a century it has
been so, and for a longer time than that it will remain as
it has been. Generations passed away have bought from
the great house, and generations yet to come will do the
same. Like the practice of honesty or truth, there is a
policy in it which brings to its adherents
PROFIT AND SATISFACTION.

‘A GOLDEN SHANTY.’
Various Verses ,
[FOR THE BULLETIN]
SHE DOES NOT KNOW: SONG WORDS.
Longtime her face has melted now
To slumbrous eyes and shadow hair—
O, mortal nature thrive and sow
Thy flickering signet everywhere !
Yet still the tyrant memory flies,
Dissolving shades before the mind,
Where surge the sheen of startled eyes
And sad lips quivering in the wind.
Let tears flow faster, lips grow pale,
Let breaking hearts beat e’er so low,
Let cowards tell their well-told tale,
She shall not know. She shall not know.
For who would tell in this worn time,
r As night shafts speed from Even’s bow,
That dull lie moulded in the prime—
I Jove you so ! I love you so !
I love you so—it stings the tongue,
And scars the ear whereon it falls —
Stretch forth and let thy heart be wrung,
And chain thy soul in prison walls.
The night goes shuddering through the stars,
The baffled winds moan to and fro,
A great heart strains its slender bars —
She does not know. She does not know.
Sydney.
JULIAN SAKE.
CRECHE SOCIETIES.
[lt was the custom, among the more “ tender-hearted ”
Jewish women of 2000 years ago, to give a cup of drugged
wine to criminals about to be crucified, in order to
deaden them to the agony of their punishment. Such a
potion was offered to Jesus of Nazareth on His way to the
cross. He, however, refused it.]
In holy charity and pious love
The wealthy women of our Christian land
Stretch out to fallen sisterhood a hand
That lifts the base-born young a step above.
A wondrous creed it is, which thus can move
Their mother-hearts, and still can let them
stand,
Unshrinking, in the robes and jewels grand
Bought with the blood and sin of yon foul drove
That herds within their tenements. E’en so
In far Judea, when that creed was born
Which now enfolds naught save hypocrisy,
Did women of two thousand years ago
Take pity on their Christ, scourged, tired, for-
lorn,
And offered Him the drug—and then the tree !
Yio.
C.P.R.
A DREAM.
Methought I was fevered and restless.
And sleep had deserted my bed ;
I rose up and walked through a city—
A city wiiose voices were dead.
Around and about me was stillness,
And I was alone in the street;
My footfall alone broke the silence—
The silence and stillness complete.
I thought : can it be that I only
Am living—the last of all men ?
I cried out: “O. God !I am lonely.
Give life to Thy creatures again.”
When out from a cloud came, in thunder,
A voice that seems near to me now ;
A voice that said ; “ I am more lonely,
Aye, even more lonely than thou.
“For even amongst all the millions
That ages and aeons have brought
To dwell in the mansions of Heaven,
There’s none like to me in my court.
“For I am the God of all ages,
Enthroned on the uppermost throne ;
No equal have I in high Heaven,
Where reign I for ever alone.”
S.
COMPROMISE.
I pencil glaring wings of Right
With Wrong’s sedater black ;
And rushing Freedom’s crotchets with
Resurgent minims slack.
I paralyse the hand of God
When He would loose at last
The cataract of vengeance, due
For age-old crimes of caste ;
For froward Duty hesitates
When wrongs grow vested rights,
And squealing Pity wards the blow
Relentless Justice smites.
The limpid clarity of Truth
I phosphoresce with lies,
And put sophistic hectics on
The pallor of the Wise.
Insipid grace of Love I lead
To Lust’s lips’ hydromel ;
Or twine the snow-cold Heaven with
The glowing limbs of hell.
Melb.
DANTON.
TO A MAORILAND ROBIN.
I may not fit the music of thy song
To any phrases of my halting measure ;
To thee, and thee alone, can this belong—
Thy love-trill’s joyous treasure.
Yet may I listen till the after-glow
Melts in the duskiness of Night’s soft tresses—
Regretful that thy sweet song’s ardent flow
Disdains the Night’s caresses.
In the sweet witchery of thy far song
A world of happy thoughts and idle dreamings
Come from the fairyland where Youth belongs,
That hath its idle gleanings.
Thou lovest Day, with all his changing moods,
And when it dies thy notes grow low and
tender —
The Night, like Death, within the shadow broods,
Thy song hath nought to lend her.
Sweet Love’s despair? or longing for thy mate?
Inspires thy minstrelsy, mayhap, with sadness ;
But Death hath nought to do with love or hate—
Nor all thy songs with gladness.
Yet, would I fain beyond the after-glow,
Ask for one moment but to hear thy singing ;
Death’s way is dark, and mortals thither go,
No sound of song there bringing.
CHRIS. CLEMENTSON.
Wellington, Maoriland.

TERMS TO SUBSCRIBERS
(Payable in Advance, only)

NOTICE.
By sending direct to Tub Bulletin Office Twenty
Shillings sterling—with postage added where required—
which you may pay by Post Office Order, One Pound
Note, or stamps of any Australian province, you can
have The Bulletin posted regularly to you every week for
a year.
This offer is not open to residents of Sydney. In that
city Tub Bulletin is not delivered by mail, but is entirely
in the hands of our carriers, with whom we do not wish to
interfere.
Cheques, Drajts, etc., and all Business Communications
to be addressed to William Macleod, Managing Director.
Literary Communications to be addressed J. P.
Archibald. •
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
‘ Correspondence ” column all contributions submitted—
tvhether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or, say, 3000 words, are specially acceptable),
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unavailable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that
we are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.

SATURDAY, MAY 18, 1895.
The Foreign-Trade Resurrection.
Premier Reid’s budget speech is supposed
to mark the beginning of another alleged
Freetrade epoch in New South Wales.
Whether it really marks the beginning of
such an epoch, or the beginning of anything
at all, no reasonable person can profess to
know. The Government programme is too
unwieldy to have any serious prospect of
success. There are several things in it that
are excellent, some that are wholly bad, and
some that are absolutely ridiculous. It is so
large and expansive that when it is brought
in by a man of Premier Reid’s calibre the
spectacle is suggestive of an ant bringing in
a bag of flour. The fact that no one has, so
far, ever been able to take Mr. Reid
seriously tells against him now more than at
any previous stage of his political career.
Even a politician of much larger dimensions
would hardly be taken seriously under like
circumstances.
# * #
Premier Reid closed the last year with an
alleged deficit of £271,000. The real deficit
was nearly £490,000, but by a beautifully
original process £216,000 —expended in the
latter part of 1894 and the beginning
of 1895—was debited to 1893 and
charged to the preceding Government. The
same financier’s deficit for the first half of
1895 is £239,000, but it is converted into a
surplus by chaiging to loans a sum of
£345,000 which his predecessors had already
paid out of revenue, and annexing this
amount out of loan-funds. This novel mode
of finance says little for the Treasurer’s
political honesty ; it is, in fact, about the
most densely stupid mode of manipulating
the public accounts yet invented. The
shortage, as is usual with all New South
Wales shortages, is to be liquidated for the
present by the issue of Treasury bills, and
passed on to posterity. Everything that is
bad is passed on to posterity in Australia.
The Treasury bills are to be paid later on by
means of the successive surpluses which are
to arise through a “sound system of finance,”
but as Mr. Reid’s sound finance has so far
only produced two deficits and no surplus at
all, this settlement looks hazy and very
far oft’. Also, it is promised, for the
several-dozenth time within a year, that
the public accounts are to be put into
such a shape as to be absolutely intelligible
to everybody. This promise, however, looks
none too brilliant in view of the incompre-
hensible manner of Mr. Reid’s accounts up
to date ; moreover, previous Treasurers have
been for many years on the point of doing
the same thing, and yet nothing has eventu-
ated. In future, according to the N.S.W.
Premier, the chaotic system of debiting
expenditure back to previous years is to
cease entirely. Which is especially good
news as coming from a Government which
has carried that evil practice to a greater
extent than any of its predecessors.
• # *
The present Government came into office
with—to use its own frequent expression—
a mandate from the country to establish a
pure and absolute system of Freetrade.
There is abundant room to doubt the wisdom
or intelligence of the country, but about the
mandate there is no possible doubt whatever
It was as definite as any such mandate could
be, and as decisive as a large majority could
make it. Under these circumstances, it is
lamentable to meet once more with the same
old disappointment which has followed every
Freetrade upheaval within the memory of
this generation. There is to be no Freetrade
after all. There are to be duties on alcohol,
tobacco in all shapes, and on narcotics. A
duty of Id. per lb. on candles is to be
retained for two years, after which time
it is to disappear gradually—if the Reid
Government happens to be still in office,
and the Houge endorses its action, and it has
the courage of its opinions. A duty is to be
levied on kerosene for another 12 months, at
the end of which period the Government
gives its unsupported assurance that it will
be removed. The duty on sugar, confec-
tionery, &c., is to remain unchanged for two
years, and then, if the Government survives
and doesn’t change its mind, and its majority
still keeps up, the country has its personal
asseveration that the evil thing will be re-
moved so gradually that it will not finally
disappear until 1902. The duties on salt,
galvanised iron, wire netting, &c., are to re-
main for 12 months, when the Reid Govern-
ment gives its political P/N, without endorse-
ment, that they will be abolished. All the
rest of the old revenue duties of the Parkes
regime are to be maintained until June, 1896,
and the grain duties levied by the Dibbs
Government are to remain for another five
months. Also, the Premier, after announcing
overnight his resolve to abolish all duties
except those specifically mentioned, pro-
ceeded next morning to collect at the'Customs
a lot of entirely new duties, mostly on the
necessaries of life, concerning which imposts
no notice whatever had been given. Under
these circumstances, even Mr. Reid’s Free-
trade revival is so far off that he and his Min-
istry can hardly expect to live until its arrival.
Possibly they don’t want to. Their desire,
apparently, is to avert their great nostrum in
their own lifetime. Considering bow long
and consistently New South Wales has
voted the Freetrade ticket, and yet
that the province has never once en-
joyed enough Freetrade to know what
that great principle is like, this renewed
disappointment is irritating. The fact
that, out of a long and dreary suc-
cession of Freetraue politicians, not one has
dared to carry out his convictions, or to come
within 100 miles of carrying out his convic-
tions, almost suggests ‘that there is some-
thing wrong with the great principle, and
that its own advocates are afraid to give it a
trial lest they should be unable thereafter to
advocate it with a semblance of decency any
longer,
• • •
The reason assigned by Mr. Reid for re-
moving certain of the existing duties on
manufactured goods is that they raise prices
and thus fall heavily on the consumer, and
the reason assigned for removing the duties
on agricultural produce is that they reduce
prices and thus descend with a sickenimr
thud on the producer. The reason assigned
for not abolishing the duty on sugar, which
is a necessary alike to rich and poor, is that
the revenue which it yields can’t be dis-
pensed with ; and the apparent reason for
abolishing the duties on silks, satins, pianos,
jewellery, precious stones, pictures, &c.,
which are mere luxuries, is that the revenue
can be dispensed with. Freetrade, accord-
ing to the Premier, is good for everybody,
yet special concessions in the way of Protec-
tion are made to the sugar, galvanised iron,
wire netting, confectionery, kerosene, candle,
and several dozen other industries by way of
“acting leniently.” Leniency, in this case,
is, therefore, apparently, an effort to prevent
the blessings of Freetrade from falling on
the people too suddenly, and a gradual re-
moval of the curse of Protection lest the
country might feel the want of it too severely.
Altogether, Premier Reid’s fiscal policy is
about the most wobblesome thing of its kind
which New South Wales has met with for a
long time, and the country’s chronic inability
to take its small, fat statesman in a serious
spirit will be considerably aggravated
thereby.
* * *
It is estimated that the alterations in the
tariff will involve a loss of £555,000 per
annum, and this is to be made up, partly by
increased legacy and succession duties—
partly by means of the laud and income-tax,
and partly by retrenchment. All these pro-
posals are eminently commendable, and so
also is the scheme for placing the Civil Ser-
vice under the control of three independent
Commissioners, with a view to eliminating
political influence and establishing the public
departments on a purely business footing.
The Premier likewise announces that ho has
made a saving of £lOO,OOO on the Civil
Service for the ensuing year, though there
are, so far, no visible signs of any such
saving on the Estimates. A large, but
rather shadowy scheme of relief for the un-
employed is announced, apparently with a
solemn conviction that the Reid policy is
not calculated in itself to relieve destitution,
and that Freetrade, as a remedy for poverty
and chronic scarcity of work, is a failure. It
is proposed to amalgamate the Post Office
and the Commissioners’ Savings Banks, a
measure which has nothing special to recom-
mend it save that it will enable stone-
broke Governments, which now secretly
borrow the funds of the former institution, to
borrow those of the latter also ; and at some
very vague and unspecified date, when the
Government is fully rested, it is suggested that
this combined institution may be made the
sole note-issuing bank of N.S. Wales. Also,
with a judicious recognition of the fact that
Freetrade in grain and other produce won’t
help the struggling agriculturist, but will
rather be a further weight upon him, it is
proposed to extend the term allowed for the
payment of selectors’ instalments from the
already liberal term of thirty-two years to
sixty-six—or two-thirds of a century.
* * *
The good features about the Financial
Statement are those which have no definite
connection with it; the bad feature about
the Statement is the Statement itself. The
proposal to create a State bank of issue, and
make the paper currency of the country a
Government monopoly, backed by the public
credit, is one which cannot be too highly
praised, and though it has no earthly con-
nection with the finances of the year, it helps
to lend to the budget a respectability which
is not its own. Unfortunately, however,
the Premier merely states on his own respon-
sibility that such a reform should be effected.
He distinctly refuses to pledge the Govern-
ment to support it ; he also does not
pledge himself to support it ; neither does
he fix a date when a bill will be intro-
duced to this effect —in fact, there is no
semblance of a promise that any such bill
will ever be introduced. Under these con-
ditions the hest feature in the Financial
Statement is too shadowy to be of any
serious account. The appointment of a
Civil Service Commission is another purely
extraneous matter, and although it is a
statesmanlike scheme (or would be, if it were
proposed to make the Commission respon-
sible to Parliament only instead of to the
Ministry of the day) and one which could
hardly fail to be an improvement on the pre-
sent chaotic system, its inclusion in the State-
ment makes the Government’s finance no
better in theimmediate present Thepostpone-
mentof selectors’arrears isseeminglya clumsy
bribe to the agricultural interest—which is
mainly Protectionist ; and the unemployed
relief scheme comes with a bad grace from
the man who proposed to “save the country ”
and abolish the necessity for such relief in
the future. As for the actual finance of the
Ministry it is, in the main, half-hearted.
New South Wales has had many alleged
Freetrade Governments, but it has never had
Freetrade. Yet it has voted for that
monstrosity so often that it deserves to get
it. The obvious duty of Mr. Reid and his
following is to remove, wholly and at
once, the duties on agricultural pro-
duce, sugar, candies, kerosene, salt, wire-
netting, and everything else. If the
needful revenue cannot, by any lawful
means, be otherwise obtained, then an
exception may possibly be made, for purely
revenue purposes, in favor of tobacco, alco-
hol, and opium, but there is no earthly
reason, save the weakness of the Premier s
political knees, why the exception should go
any further. The advantages of having a
Freetrade majority and a Freetrade Govern-
ment, if the country has not, and is not
going to have, Freetrade, are not very ap-
parent. Also, The Bulletin, at all events,
is tired of a system whereby an alleged
Freetrade Government runs a semi-Protec-
tive tariff, and if things go well the
Freetrade Government is the cause, and if
they go ill the Protective tariff is responsible.
In short, this paper wants a Government
which will go in definitely and conscien-
tiously and entirely for either Freetrade or
Protection, or else will say distinctly that it
commits itself to neither policy and claims
the right to be guided by circumstances. So
far, however, New South Wales has only
had Protectionist Governments which did
not protect, and Freetrade Governments
which did not set trade free, and similar
anomalies.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Newspaper Postage.
New South Wales Treasurer Retd omits from
his financial programme one easily-imposed tax
which would yield the province perhaps £25,000
per annum. That is a postage-fee of |d. each
on newspapers carried within the province. All
other Australasian provinces take this revenue :
there is no good reason why New South Wales
should refuse it. Every morning special trains
labour out of Sydney to do nothing but carry
the metropolitan dailies free of charge into the
country. If a man starts a grocery business,
and sets out to swamp the back-blocks with his
particular brand of soap and candles, the rail-
way authorities charge him freight as a matter
of course. It is recognised that the State does
not borrow money to build railways, and the
taxpayers do not sweat to pay the interest on
the borrowed money, in order that one man
may make large profits out of soap and candles.
But when a man starts a newspaper business,
and begins to collect back-block pennies in
exchange for his particular brand of politics
and his particular variety of prejudices, the
State immediately pays* a' large staff of men
wages, and incurs heavy expenditure on rail-
way rolling-stock, in order to make his divi-
dends larger. Free postage means the gain of
thousands a year to*, the S.M. Herald,
Sydney Telegraph and other metropolitan
journals. Why should the proprietors of
newspapers be subsidised by the State
simply because they have invested their
money in newspapers instead of in drapery
or ironmongery ? Why should those who
clamor so loudly fur freetrade receive
so much protection —protection at the ex-
pense of the country press, which is quite
as well fitted to disseminate truth and light
as its city rivals, yet is starved and out-at-
elbows because Govt, gives the naturally well-
handicapped city man the additional handicap
of free postage ? Of course, newspapers are
the voice of progress, the vehicle of knowledge;
but they are not published solely to promote
knowledge and progress. Their first object is
a cash return to their proprietors ; and, if the
fine ideals conflict with that object, the fine
ideals may go hang. Anyhow, people who can
afford to pay a private speculator for supplying
them with news, can afford to pay Govt, for
supplying the supply. When “Shakespeare”has
to pay postage John Fairfax and Sons ought
to pay, too. So ought The Bulletin, as this
paper cheerfully admits. The Bulletin doesn’t
want the assembly of taxpayers to put hands
in their pockets to carry it to its subscribers ;
and it cordially invites Treasurer Reid to make
it pay postage—and every other newspaper as
well.
• ■*
The Coalition.
The very latest move in N.S.W. politics is the
coalition between the Protectionist Opposition
and that scrag-end of the nondescript faction
which is led by Mr. Henry Parkes. For the
time, at least, G. Republican Dibbs stands out
from his position of chief (one can’t be Premier
and Agent-General at the same time), and the
unholy alliance is to make a rush for the loaves
and fishes, under Parkes’s leadership. If the
attack succeeds, then Parkes, the Daddy
Christmas of N.S.W. politics, the boss Free-
trader and Kiama Ghost fanatic, will be
Premier of a Protectionist Ministry, with the
old Protectionist party as his principal support.
The semi-Protective Dibbs tariff will remain-
in that respect the Protectionists appear to got
the best of the deal; and the new Parkes
Ministry will come to the front with a great
whoop for Federation ; and Parkes will state
that he doesn’t adopt the Protectionist tariff
because he has changed his views, for he is the
same consistent Parkes as aforetime, nor be-
cause he wants office, because he returns to
power with extreme reluctance ; but simply be-
cause Australian unity is of such vast import-
ance that everything else is trivial by compari-
son. In which case, Mr. H. Parkes will speak
the truth by accident. The whole proceeding
is a miserable case of folly, greed, bad general-
ship, blundering, and insensate muddle. If
Parkes wins, he gets office. If he doesn’t
win, he loses nothing. He has been so
many things in his time that he may be-
come Protectionist, Mahommedan, Theosophist,
Spiritualist, Roman Catholic, vegetarian, or
dog-worshipper in his old age without becoming
any more grotesque than he already is. But if
Parkes wins, the Protectionists gain the eternal
disgrace of having thrown over their leade‘r and
allied themselves with their worst enemy,
chiefly for the sake of a few portfolios and”a
little official salary, aud if Parkes doesn’t win,
they gain the same disgrace without any cor-
responding advantage. They don’t, it is true,
wholly surrender their principles for the sake
of office, for the Dibbs tariff is to be maintained
so far as it goes, but they agree to forego any
attempt at genuine Protection, for the time at
all events, and that ignoble compromise is at
least a partial surrender of principle. But, even
if there was no compromise at all, and no sur-
render of principle whatever, the Protectionist-
party can’t afford to associate itself with
Parkes. No party, in fact, can afford to asso-
ciate itself with Parkes. And if Premier Reid
is defeated and obtains a dissolution, arid forces '
the Protectionist-party to go to the country
under the virtual guidance of Parkes, its situa-
tion will be ridiculous and wholly pitiable.
Moreover, the entire business is done at
the wrong time. The country put G. H.
Reid in office with a. large majority, and
he should be left to prove his incapacity
to its fullest extent. So far, he has been de-
feated six or eight times; his Local Government
Bill has been lost; his Land and Income-tax Bill
has been altered in many of its leading features
simply because of his inability to write plain
English and do simple arithmetic; his two
financial statements have been cooked in the
most childish fashion ; his promised Freetrade
tariff consists in promising to remove a number
of duties in a dim future which extends as fat
as six years ahead, and in putting on certain
other duties in the immediate present without
even informing Parliament of his intention ;
and his economies consist largely in promising
to default to the extent of =8200,000 in the pay-
ments to the public creditor. Another year or
so of this unspeakable mismanagement would
make an end of G. H. Reid and his alleged
Freetrade party for a generation to come.
The attempt to eject him before he has time to
show his own hopeless futility, and the hopeless
futility of his alleged policy, is simply cutting
down the partially hanged enemy before he is
dead, and leaving him to resuscitate for future
use. The country decided that it wanted G.
H. Reid, and it should be allowed to have him
until it decities that it will never want him any
more. The Bulletin, in the cause of Federa-
tion and Protection, votes for Reid’s continu-


ance in office. Nothing will do so much good
for either cause as Reid’s continuance in office.
Government by Contradiction.
The Reid (N.S.W.) Government was defeated
the other night by a crushing majority of 36
votes against 16 upon its little, unobtrusive
I>road Rill, and the Bill itself disappeared in
consequence. It was also twice badly defeated
over the Local Government Bill—one of the
most important measures on its political pro-
gramme, which was also cast into outer dark-
ness ; and before this it was defeated several
times on various details connected with the
same Bill, and several more times on other
topics of more or less importance. The Reid
Government goes on the even tenor of its way
regardless of all these defeats. And the Sydney
Freetrade papers, which denounced the shame-
lessness and mercenary spirit and wicked
office-grabbing of the previous Ministry for
holding its ground after one small reverse, have
not, so far, even hinted that it is time for
the present crowd to retire from the scene.
As a matter of fact, the Reid Cabinet
is fully justified in holding on, and the preced-
ing Dibbs Cabinet—which hung on much less
tenaciously—was also justified. The country
presumably wanted Reid as Premier, else it
would not have returned him to office, and as
the Assembly refuses to throw him out on a
definite motion of censure it appears that it
still wants him, so far as its ideas on the sub-
ject can be ascertained. The fact that the
country, or the Assembly, wants Premier Reid
in no way implies, however, that it wants his
Bread Bill, or his particular brand of Local
Government, and it should be at full liberty to
keep him and to reject or amend them at its
own discretion. It should also be at liberty, if
it so desires, to keep him while rejecting
or amending every * other item in his
political programme, or to keep him while
rejecting any or all of his colleagues, or to keep
his colleagues while rejecting him, or to do any-
thing else it pleases. The party system, by
which the Legislature has to accept the whole
Ministry and the whole Ministerial programme
or else reject the lot, is mere foolishness ; and
the new departure of the Reid Government,
which allows the Assembly to reject or alter its
bills but refuses to quit without direct in-
structions to that effect, is the beginning of a
change for the better. Whether the present
New South Wales Cabinet inaugurated the new
method out of weakness, or greed of office, or
a patriotic desire to amend an exceedingly
foolish political system, or through a mere ten-
dency to drift, is a mere side - issue. The
method itself is an improvement, and the
motive is the Cabinet’s own affair.
How Thing-s Don’t Happen
In Victoria, a few days ago, a girl of 17 laid an
information for rape against her father, a
State-school teacher. She stuck to her ghastly
tale for some little time, and then broke up and
acknowledged that it was a pure invention
from beginning to end ! The only reason for
her astounding lie was that the old man had
strongly objected to her “ walking out” with a
young man. So far, there is no news of the
arrest for gross perjury of this dangerous
woman, who is certainly unfit to be at large.
Neither has The Bulletin ever heard of the
arrest of the girl who swore falsely against
Buttner a good many years ago, and brought
him within seven days’ march of the gallows.
Nor has it heard of the arrest of any one of a
dozen females who laid criminal charges against
different men after the Mount Rennie episode,
all which charges proved to be utterly falsej
and most of them the result of unsuccessful
efforts at blackmail. It is an unwritten law in
Australia that any female may bring the most
horrible charges against any man, and even
though they are proved to be pure invention,
she will go free. Also, it would really
appear to be another unwritten law that,
no matter how many such females are
bowled out, Judges shall sit down indig-
nantly on any barrister who expresses a doubt
as to the absolute purity and reliability of the
next female. At Ohiltern a girl of 18 years
recently charged two men with having
criminally assaulted her, but having under
cioss-examination tick now lodged that her evi-
deuce - in-chief was untrue, and that if the
prisoners had given her money as they pro-
mised she would not have made the charges,
the accused were discharged. At Warrnambool*
the same week a girl, who complained of having
been outraged by two men who were arrested
for the offence, was clearly shown to be a 24-
carat liar, but nothing happened to her except
that the bench discharged her intended prey.
Also, nothing happened to the Ohiltern per-
jurer. Nothing ever happens, in fact.
Straining- at a Gnat.
Melbourne’s respectable Argus is up in arms
against an alleged case of Bench-packing at
Oollingwood police-court. It was not an ordi-
nary case, such as occurs a hundred times per
annum in Victoria without fetching a ghost of
a groan from the Freetrade organ. This was a
revolting instance —so the AkguS says—of
certain “ Trades-hall justices” packing the
Bench in the interests of “ union pickets who
were summoned for misbehavior.” The dis-
graceful pickets escaped condign punishment;
all the charges against them were dismissed,
wherefore the Argus calls upon the Ministry to
bundle the offending magistrates, neck and
ciop, off the Bench as an example.” The
Bulletin begs to second the resolution. It is
time that a batch of men were bundled off
the Bench as an example, and even though
the Trades’-hall justices might not answer the
purpose of a shocking example, they ought to
go. Then the public would quickly discover
reasons why the whole crowd of honorary
magistrates ought to be bundled off likewise,
tor the sake of common decency, and these, in
their turn, might “split upon” the paid ben Its,
and direct attention to bad Judges ; and
' I( d°ria, after all, might become quite a clean
and wholesome abiding place. The curse of
the country, thus far, is that nobody of im-
portance gets thrown out neck and crop for
doing anything. As for lawyers, they are out-
sule the pale of retribution altogether.
At a Melbourne attorney were to mur-
der a member of another ptofession—say
% U (A 10nist P icket “-he would escape with a fine
ot 40s. or a month. If he were to murder a
rival lawyer, or slaughter a learned brother in
cold blood, that would be more awkward. The
t all Court would no doubt discuss the case and
take up a compromise. But we don’t hear the
Ahijus singing out in this strain. A rotten
state of affairs seems quite good enough for a
great ’ newspaper until Freetrader particular
om lre , to pufc (lown union Pickets is baulked by
Irades-hall justices.” Then is the time for
tc country to shake the dew-drops from its
mane and protect the interests of cheap labor.
The “ Opening- Far North.
Mr. Dashwood, Government Resident in the
-Northern territory, announces it as his con-
viction that “ the only feasible scheme for
opening up” that # great Land of Desolation is
a transcontinental railway on the land-
grant system. This yearning to “open up”
new territory is a complaint which needs to be
watched. At present Australia has more land
opened up to a greater or less extent than her
population can inhabit. It wouldn’t pay to open
up the Northern Territory in the usual fashion,
because there is not sufficient population there
to support a railway, and one reason why there
is not sufficient population is that settlement
happened to begin at the other end of Aus-
tralia, and the country hasn’t yet grown enough
people to spread over the intervening spaces,
ihe population and the railway will get there
about the same time, and the Territory will be
opened up in the ordinary course of events
just as soon as it is wanted, and no sooner.
Also, when the population gets there, steam-
ship hues will begin to patrol the Northern
coast, and call in at all the ports there
as regularly as they now do at Sydney
or Melbourne. At present, sea communication
is scarce for the same reason that land com-
munication is non-existent—there is little or
nothing to support it. The proposal to give
away a vast area of land, which will be badly
required by-and-bye, in order to open up a dis-
trict which doesn’t need to be opened for the
next twenty years at least, and to support a
railway which isn t wanted, and to bring in
people to settle on the land just when the State
has given away all its land to a railway
syndicate, and has nothing left to settle
them on, is an upside-down mode of pro-
cedure. Moreover, even if the land was
“ opened-up ”by a transcontinental line, and
a great body of settlers arrived to grow crops
there, they couldn’t possibly send their produce
away by that line, for the simple reason that it
wouldn’t pay for the cost of transit, and the
great railway would be the worst white ele-
phant in Australia. Until this country can
raise 2,000,000 people to inhabit the Northern
ierritoiy which it can’t do at present—it
had better be left unopened. The Territory
will be wanted badly enough half a century
hence, and when it is wanted it will open itself
up. The attempt to seize population by the
scruff of the neck and run it violently into
places where at present it doesn’t want to go is
always a failure.
The Sydney Foreign-trade press expresses its
scornful wonder at the very apparent fact that
there is hardly a prominent Protectionist in
Victoria who is not an ardent Australian
lederationist, and who - is not vigorous in his
demand for the removal of barriers to inter-
provincial Freetrade. Your Freetrader never
can understand a practical result that does not
coincide with a sketched theory. Inter-
provincial Freetrade is part of the very
essence and nature of Australian Protec-
tion. 1 lie difference between Freetrade
and Protection is that the first is dogmatic
and the second is eclectic. One is a “ school
the other is a living adaptation. Freetrade is,
asserted to be an absolute verity, incapable of
shock from any rude circumstance whatever—
a cure-all—a remedy for anything, from com-
mercial leprosy to trade squint. Protection, on
the other hand, is the particular expediency that
is deemed to be the wisest under conditions as
they arise and circumstances as they occur.
Protection can conceive of a land and a people
being so situated that their natural and most
beneficial policy would be Freetrade. Freetrade
cannot conceive of any land and any people
being so circumstanced that they would need
protection. Protection thus takes geographical
and climatic conditions, and especially geogra-
phical homogeneity, into consideration ; and it
is owing to this necessity of Protection that
Australian Protection has as its “square root,”
so to speak, interprovincial Freetrade. So long
as the provinces were, and are, contentiously
apart, so long was, and is, interprovincial Free-
trade no factor at all in their individual
policies. Victorian Protection took no thought
of it until New South Wales Protection
presented the retaliatory reverse of the shield.
It is safe to say that New South Wales Protec-
tion has effected more than any other single
cause in converting Victorian Protectionists “to
interprovincial Freetrade. And, with a unified
Australia, there can be no other possible policy.
For then will not only border duties be abol-
ished, but, in many senses, borders also.
The other day, in a Melbourne police-court, an
aged and infirm woman was brought up on a
charge of vagrancy. Her son, a fairly well-to-
do man, appeared in court, and when, in giving
his evidence, he was asked whether the accused
was his mother, he replied that she was “in a
sort of a way,” which qualification he after-
wards explained as meaning that he was her
illegitimate offspring. The bench thereupon
told him that he should have been ashamed of
himself to have mentioned such a matter, and
upon his further declining to take over and
keep the poor old woman some more invectives
were thrown at him and he left the court.
There can be no two opinions as to whether
this man deserved the reflections of the Bench
or not; but the question arises whether, a man
being liable to the disadvantages of illegitimacy,
he is not logically entitled to its advantages.
Probably the humane and delicately-minded
person in the foregoing report had been
throughout his life subjected to all manner of
taunts and jeers from the ultra-respectable por-
tion of the country until he had become utterly
callous to them. Had the mother been heiress
to any legacy and died intestate he would
have received not a penny thereof, and in a
number of families his “birth-stain” would
have been an effectual bar to his marriage with
one of its daughters. Therefore, having had
to contend against all these opposing forces from
which the legitimate are free, he certainly
appears entitled to some consideration when he
asks to be relieved of the moral obligation
which humanity imposes upon the healthy
young to support their parents in their old age.
Some of the evidence given re the Yarra tunnel
disaster seems to put an even uglier comnlexion
on that event than it previously wore. As
deduced from the report of the inquest the
story reads very much as follows : The ground
was known to be extremely treacherous, and
had to be kept up by air-pressure. When the
air-lock was left open then the door leading
into the face was firmly closed by the pressure,
and escape was impossible. The air-lock was
left open for half-an-hour at a time, and was
open at the time of the fatality. The door
leading into the face might possibly have been
pushed open by a great combined effort from
outside, and the imprisoned men enabled to
escape, but the effort wasn’t made because two
trucks had bepn left blocking the road. There
was, possibly, just a shadow of a chance that
the air-pressure in the chamber where the men
were working might have partially resisted the
inflow of water and given the victims a
slight hope of escape, but somebody opened
the valve in an effort to fill the lock,
and forgot to shut it again, and thus
let the air escape, and put a summary
end to this possibility. Nobody was kept close
to the door to watch for signals, in case
anything did go wrong with the men at work
in the death-trap. There was apparently no
definite code of communication, for when the
men at the face made frantic signs through
the glass pane that they wanted to get out their
gesticulations were interpreted to mean that
they wanted someone else to come in. And
all this cheerful and easy-going recklessness
was carried on despite the fact that for two or
three weeks there had been a considerable
inflow of water through the rotten strata over-
head, which was a tolerably clear proof that a
bigger inflow might come in at any moment.
Yet the contractors were rushed with men
anxious to toil under these gruesome conditions
for 7s. a day, and despite this fact the Fat Man
still rises to declare, from behind Ids abundant
white vest, that the “unemployed” are mere
loafers who won’t work when the chance pre-
sents itself.
At Katoomba, the other day, Chief Justice
Harley said, amongst other things, that
“British justice would always be found im-
partial.” The pronouncement sounds well, but
does it mean that the Dean trial was an im-
partial one, or that the Mount Rennie trial was
a sample of British justice ? As Chief Justice,
Acting-Gov. Darley must be held in no small
degree responsible for the effect of two exhibi-
tions which have brought one division of the
N.S. Wales Supreme Court into odium with
all honest citizens, and have degraded Australian
justice in the eyes of the world. If Chief
Justice Darley, figure-head of the New South
Wales superior Bench, and official protector
of the corporate dignity thereof, cannot
initiate measures to prevent his Court being
brought into general disrepute, he had better
retire to Dublin. His predecessor, Chief Justice
Martin, would not have allowed the honor and
prestige of the Supreme Court to be thus
dragged into the mire by the unseemly
vagaries of any wearer of the ermine. Never
in Judge Martin’s day were there witnessed
such disgraceful spectacles, involving the degra-
dation and humiliation of the chief jurisdiction
of the supreme tribunal of the province.
A Perth draper—one of the hated crowd of
“ other-siders ” —had qualified by a six months’
residence as a Parliamentary elector. At the
time when he tilled in his application, however,
he was about to remove to another house in the
same electorate, and in the application he set
down his qualification as six months’ residence
at the new address instead of the old one. As
his removal did not invalidate his claim, the
act could not possibly be a fraudulent scheme
to get a vote to which he was not entitled ; he
had an equal right to the vote whether he re-
moved or not, and no man could fairly be sus-
pected of an 'attempt to dishonestly acquire
what ho already possessed by honest means.
Nevertheless, a typically pig-headed VVestralian
jury found him guilty of fraud on the strength
of this mis-statement, and the Chief-Justice,
after a solemn deliverance about the enormity
of his offence, “ which quite amounted to per-
jury,” sentenced him to 12 months’ hard labor.
A huge petition, urging his release, is now being
prepared for presentation to the Acting-Gover-
nor, but as the Acting-Governor is the same
Chief-Justice who passed the monstrous sen-
tence, the draper’s prospects amount to very
little. Apropos, will somebody tell The
Bulletin by what means the 7th section of
the N.S. W. Charter of Justice has been ren-
dered null and void? The section in question
expressly provides that the Chief Justice shall
not occupy any other paid office whatsoever.
Despite the vigorous remonstrances of the Yic.
railway department, a number of ex-M.’sL.A.
—some of them individuals who were sacked
by their constituents long, long ago—still
refuse to give up their free railway passes. The
department, therefore, applied to the Crown
Solicitor for a high-class legal opinion as to
whether it could lay an information against any
of these individuals when found travelling on
their gold tokens, and the Crown Solicitor fur-
nished the staggering information that no pro-
ceedings could be taken unless it could be
shown that the act was done “ with intent to
defraud.” This amazingdoctrinerequires a lot of
hard thinkingover. A member’s pass is available
while he is a member and no longer. If ho
travels on it when he has ceased to he a member
then he is, so far as the ordinary lay intelli-
gence can see, a common swindler travelling on
an expired ticket and refusing to pay the legal
fare. When any ordinary, non-legislative
person does that it is f|uite needless to prove
that he does it “with intent to defraudthe
law is satisfied with the plain fact that he is
defrauding, and sends him to gaol there and
then. According to the Crown Solicitor’s
dogma, however, the ex-member may visibly
defraud the revenue as much.as he likes, pro-
viding it isn’t proved that he defrauds the
revenue with intent to defraud the revenue. And
the fact that he is defrauding the revenue, after
the most solemn warnings, is not to be taken as
sufficient evidence that he means to defraud the
revenue. Should this paper ever be charged
before a Victorian court with a clear case of
wilful murder, it proposes to set up the defence
that it can’t be legally convicted unless it is
proved that it killed the deceased with intent
that he should be dead. It is a very poor legal
opinion that won’t work at least two ways.
About the only bright idea the Australasian
Bench ever had was that the defendant should
be compelled to marry the girl. A fine example
of this eternal resolve to make him marry the
girl at all costs recently transpired in Maori-
land. Ihe defendant had been ordered to pay
7s. 6d. weekly towards.the support of his alleged
illegitimate child. He appealed to the superior
court, and for hours the hall of that august
tribunal was filled with hard and profuse swear-
ing as to the alleged paternity of the infant.
Finally, the Judge upheld the previous decision,
and decided that defendant had been guiltv of
voluminous perjury, and then defendant was
practically given the option of marrying the
girl or being prosecuted for false-swearing.'
Whether he married the girl or not this paper
doesn’t know, but the system by which a
Judge or magistrate first decides that the
defendant is a soulless and objectionable seducer
and perjurer, and then attempts to rush a
girl into becoming his partner for life, is brutal
and wholly ridiculous. Moreover, if perjury
can be condoned by marrying the girl against
whom the offence was committed, why shouldn’t
the same rule apply to other offences? The
Bulletin cordially recommends this view of
the case to the Bench’s consideration. Some
day it hopes to hear an Australian “ beak ”
deliver himself as follows: “Prisoner at the
bar, you have been found guilty of brutally
assaulting this girl, who was a complete stranger
to you, robbing her, and breaking her leg in
two places. Unless you marry her, the sentence
will be seven years’ penal servitude.” And
thereupon the prisoner will express his willing-
ness to marry her on the spot, and the case will
be dismissed with the magisterial blessing.
Premier Reid proposes to put his Civil Service
Commissioners under the thumb of the Execu-
tive Council— i.e., under the thumb of the
Ministers whose patronage they are supposed
to govern ! The plan has been tried in Queens-
land ; and the result was that, after a brief
trial of strength, the Commissioners knuckled
under to the Cabinet and became mere salaried
figureheads-which they are to this day, with
Ministerial patronage hardly checked. Reid’s
Commissioners must be able, honest men, paid
highly enough to be absolutely independent,
and responsible to Parliament only, like the
New South Wales Railway Commissioners.
The whole controversy as to re-union of the
Christian churches is hollow. The truth of the
signs of the times is that the English people,
who are slowly beginning to really govern Eng-
land, mean disestablishment of the Church of
England-mean to haul off the Old Man of
the Sea who has bestraddled the State in the
name of a theolatry which declares that the
Head of Christ’s Church on Earth is heredi-
tary ; and that the Monarch of the State
is also Pope of the Church. For, in this
little matter, there isn’t a pin to choose between
Anglicanism and Papalism. It is an even more
daring assumption to call a hereditary line of
princes and princesses heaven-born Church
Autocrats than it is to call an elected Cardinal
the Vicar of Cod. The English people are sick
of the pretensions of the “Church by Law
Established.” They groan under the burdens
laid on them by the State Pharisees and Sad-
ducees. And Romish prelates mistake the
reactionary trend of the English people against
State Churchism for an affectionate impulse
towards Rome. This is why the more shrewd
of the controversialists on their side look rather
for converts from the masses than from the theo-
logical experts of Anglicanism. Summed up,
however, these agitations are merely desperate
endeavours to stir up the ignorant into still
more ignorant emotionalism. The whole bias of
civilisation is towards agnosticism.
Editor Bulletin,—l was a steerage passen-
ger per steamer from Brisbane to Sydney the
other day. One morning, just after the watch
had been relieved at 4 o’clock, I saw the look-out
man come away from his post on the fo’c’s’le-
head. He came under the fo’c’s’le, where I
was. I asked him, “ What are you at ?” He
said, “ The mate sent me to crush holystone.”
I asked him, “Is there anyone on the look-
out ?” He said, “No; but the mate told me
he would keep a look-out.” The mate was then
on the bridge. During the voyage I saw the
three mates on many occasions take the wheel
from the men in charge, while these went away
painting and doing odd jobs. I ask you, Mr.
Editor, first, can a mate do his own duty and
that of the look-out-man or wheel-man as well ?
I ask you, secondly, whether the foregoing
facts shed any light on the numerous wrecks of
Australian passenger vessels?—E. Y. Lowry.
Moreton Frewen, in his debate with Max
Hirsch, exhibited none of that statistical lucidity
which delighted his audience at Melb. Trades’
Hall, on his first appearance in Australia. Single-
taxmen were in great force at the debate, and
howled with joy at Max’s triumphant strut across
the stage. Max’s points were mostly cotton, and
were appropriately backed by a Lancashire yell.
Poor Frewen had to face an unsympathetic audi-
ence as well as to contend with a crafty, practised
and brilliant debater.

At Sydney Lyceum, “ The Mountebanks ”
was revived for three nights before half-audiences.
“The Mountebanks ” is not a very attractive opera,
and last week it was no more attractive than it is.
The music is rather flat and mechanical, and the
dialogue is Gilbert’s second-best shading off into
his third-best. The only spontaneous laugh comes
when the “Put a penny in the slot ” duet is sung
by the figures of Hamlet and Ophelia with an
abundance of grotesque wooden gesture. Lauri
was last week more than usually wooden ; and Ida
Osborne (always a very valuable auxiliary) was
exhilaratingly grotesque.
* * * *
“ Madame Angot” has been doing a very
successful season at the same theatre, but as its
career ends on Friday night, a paper which doesn’t
come out until Thursday has very little encourage-
ment to spread itself on the subject. And as
“Dorothy,” which commences on Saturday night,
ends on Tuesday and this paper doesn’t come out
again till the Thursday after, there is very little
encouragement to spread itself on that subject
either. Moreover, the “Vicar of Bray,” which
follows “Dorothy,” doesn’t start till Wednesday
night, after the next issue has gone to press, and
is gone again before Saturday, so that The Bul-
letin doesn’t see any chance of making a few nice
remarks about it, and will probably pass it by
altogether. As regards “ Madame Angot,” it is
really surprising how much novelty the opera co.
contrived to get out of that rather aged produc-
tion. Misses Nellie Stewart and Florence Young,
as the two goddesses of the essentially feminine
production, managed somehow to create an im-
pression that the play was entirely new, and the
former, in a skirt which was split nearly to the
waist and a joyous revelation of tights underneath,
was a vision which could easily have filled the
Lyceum for six weeks instead of one. “ Madame
Angot” was one delirious triumph for the win-
some Nellie, and her rent skirt and her tights ;
for though the skirt of Florence Young was rent
in a similar fashion, her tights, for some unex-
plained reason, did not catch on to any great ex-
tent. Madame Emily Soldene, as Amaranthe,
the market woman, scored a riotous success in her
one good scene. Wallace Brownlow’s voice
showed to the best advantage, though otherwise
Larivaudiere seemed rather stiff and somewhat
encumbered with his own legs. Tapley, as Ange
Pitou, was a modified success, and the larking
police-agent of Arthur Lissant deserves to be re-
membered as the best make-up in the opera, and
a person whose lurk almost amounted to genius.
* * « *
The familiar “Meg, the Castaway,” now
being revived at Sydney Royal, is a play which
seems to have been built round Maggie Moore.
Maggie, with her dances and her coster song, and
her melody about the curl of baby’s hair, and her
other song, “ Won’t you be my Sweetheart ?” and
her tears and general diablerie, is the beginning,
middle and end of the show. The stolen child
and the philanthropist with his itinerant coffee-
stall, and the saveloy man, and the dog-fancier
are mere adjuncts, and nobody really cares for
them when Miss Moore is about. All these mis-
cellaneous parts are well filled, so far as The Bul-
tktin can remember, but it doesn’t really matter
much whether they are well filled or not. The
pathos of the drama is rather cheap and doggy at
times, and the atmosphere of Shoreditch or
Whitechapel is not particularly cheerful, and
“ Meg,” as a whole, is pervaded by a faint aroma
of pig’s-trotters and two-penn’orth of gin, but,
despite all these circumstances, the revival is a
success—or ought to be, which is the same thing.
On further consideration, however, this paper
decides that it isn’t the same thing or anything
distantly resembling it.
# * * *
“ Work and Wages ” still booms very
cheerfully at Her Majesty's (Sydney), and the
public evidently likes it. The Bulletin, how-
ever, hasn’t quite decided whether it shares this
sentiment or not. Meanwhile, several things
strike it forcibly, in addition to the things which
struck it last week. One is the fact that almost
every act ends with a girl rushing out and point-
ing the finger of haggard denunciation at the
real criminal. Another is that the murderous
foreigner seems to escape from prison in each
act, and nobody ever attempts to recapture
him. Its opinion of the villain’s mental
capacity has fallen heavily since last week
through further consideration of that black-
hatted monstrosity. The villain has two wives,
and being anxious to marry a third one, with
i 525,000 a year, he intends to “ remove ” the first,
and to prove that the second is not his wife,
because at the time of their marriage the first was
still living. If the second lady makes trouble,
however, he intends to prove that she is his wife,
the first marriage having been illegal. Obviously
in one case he will be “jugged ” for bigamy, and
in the other he will be handicapped with a visible
wife, who will be a serious obstacle in the way of
his new marriage, so that he is a very dull
and wooden villain at the best. Also, The
Bulletin seriously urges Mr. Rignold to
do something with the trial scene. The
spectacle of Paul Radford being convicted
because a man, who isn’t sworn and doesn’t give
his name, rashes in with an alleged certificate,
which isn’t even attested, setting forth that Adele
Langlois, who is dead and nob very clearly
identified, was married to Geoffry Armadale,
whom nobody attempts to prove is Paul Radford,
is very much mixed. Also, the accused’s left eye
is out of drawing, and the lawyer’s statement
that the chief witness couldn’t possibly own the
bloodstained knife because his name is on it looks
illogical, and the trial isn’t a fair one owing to
the blank and awful imbecility of the prisoner’s
counsel, who has a retreating chin and a watery
eye and a forehead that goes backwards as sud-
denly as the Federation movement. Lastly, it is
an obvious mistake to turn the limelight on Mr,
Sweeney, the limelight being intended for Mr.
Rignold only. The management is _ cordially
invited to sit down somewhere on a quiet moun-
tain-side and think over these obvious discrep-
ancies.
* * * *
Mr. Livingston Hopkins (The Bulletin’s
own particular “ Hop.”) will shortly take a vaca*
tion of two or three months, during which he
intends lecturing all round Australia, the well-
known and popular L. J. Lohr being manager of the
establishment. This more or less ill-assorted pair
will commence in Melbourne some time next
month, probably at the Athenaeum Hall. “Hop.,”
who is already tolerably well-known as a humorous
lecturer and writer, as well as an artist, will
illustrate his own lectures, the pictures—-mostly
colored —being reproduced by a process of his own,
which will avoid all necessity for suspending pro-
ceedings while the lecturer has a hurried dis-
agreement with the lantern-man, after the
manner of the gifted Blathwayt. “ Hop.”
wishes it to bo distinctly understood that
he isn’t going for his health, nor for the
express purpose of conferring a blessing on the
public, nor because he supposes the public
is yearning to hear him. The great mass of the
public, he is fully aware, not having yet heard
him, doesn't care a straw whether it hears him or
not, but after it has once heard him, it will begin
to want to hear him very badly indeed. One
principal object of his tour is to see every
important person and every person who isn’t
important but thinks he is, and gather up
information for future use; and, apart from
that, Mr. Livingston H. is going lecturing
because it darned well pleases him to do so.
He pledges himself not to appear on the platform
with a johnny stick, nor to say “haw,” nor to
allude to any monarchs that have had the honor
of meeting him, nor to tell any of Talraage’s old
jokes, nor to attempt to discourse upon a fiddle
that is manifestly beyond his powers. And with
these good resolutions he goes forth confidently,
his confidence being tempered only by a fear that
the man who is entrusted in his absence with the
job of drawing Parkes may make a mess of that
statesman’s mane, and thus bring discredit on this
valuable and widely-read paper.

’Arry Rickards returned to Sydney Tivoli
a few days ago after an absence of ten months,
and was received with a storm of applause which
nearly blew the back wall out of the building.
The returned favourite sang all through the evening
more or less, until his voice threatened to gi ve out,
whereupon his enthusiastic admirers at last left off
encoring, though with extreme reluctance. “Me
an’ ’Er—’Er an’ Me” took especially well, but the
present writer takes serious exception to the
allegation in one of Mr. Rickards’ other songs :
“To err is human ; to forgive divine.” After
hearing the gifted Blathwayt “ er ” and “haw”
for a whole evening, he hereby records his convic-
tion that to “er” is absolutely inhuman, and
should be made a capital offence, and to forgive
the man who says “ er ” is merely encouraging sin.
* * * *
“In Town,” at Melb. Princess’s, has ful-
filled its first night’s promise. This festive show is
very generally preferred to the daintier thing which
paved the way for it, and Louis Brad field has
caught an admiring city by the wool. Neverthe-
less, The Bulletin’s present opinion remains
faithful to “ A Gaiety Girl,” in which there was
no special “star” part, or resurrectionary effect.
Mr. Brad field is a breezy caricature of an insol-
vent (printed “insolent” in last issue) captain,
and an artist with several sides to his talent. But
when Duchess Phelps tells him to say “Your
Grace,” and he —the supposed man about town-
replies “Pm not hungry,” Bradfield is not
humorous. At such moments, he is merely a
burlesque laugh-maker, heaving the old reliable
gags at poor untutored savages. Miss Deoima
Moore and Charles Ryley do a good deal for “ In
Town ” without getting lavishly rewarded for
their trouble. Ryley, who is said to be in ill-
health, sings accordingly, yet his gentle represen-
tation of the duke is a finished piece of work. He
has a paternal affection for Miss Decima, and
when that dear little creature discovers that he is
quite harmless in his pursuit, and acknowledges
his kindness to her beat Johnnie by letting the
old gent kiss her maiden brow, the touch of nature
makes the whole male congregation kin with Duke
Ryley.
# • *
Miss Florence Lloyd, as the duke’s son, is
also a bright, convincing young fellow, whose
image lingers in one’s memory after the curtain
falls, likewise the call-boy (Kaye) deserves a
paean of praise for his strong rendering of a
feeble song. Moat of the songs are feeble,
but two of them have choruses that the audience
can’t understand, and, as somebody remarks
during the evening, an unintelligible chorus never
fails. Miss Palotta has now adopted a skirt
instead of tights, but the tapering extremities of
Miss Maud Hobson were still in evidence at time
of writing. Otherwise Miss Hobson is not
worthily displayed by her small character,
although the fact that she is actually there playing
“principal boy” at the Ambiguity Theatre, and
can’t meet a certain tall gentleman at the stage-
door, “ because her husband has unexpectedly
returned, and she will have to go home that even-
ing”—well, the fact is of vast importance. Mel-
bourne loves her under any circumstances. She is
a sort of human Naples. Yon see her, and die-
having taken a look at Miss Blanche Massey in
the sweet meantime. “In Town ” was originally
billed for a 12-nights run, but it seems to have got
the lease extended. “The Shop Girl” may not
be wanted just yet.
* * *
“ The Span of Life ” has achieved a fifth
week at the Royal, and probably thinks of retir-
ing soon. It is a wonderful drama as regards
plot, dialogue, scenery, and sublime impossibility
all round, but it can’t last for ever. Besides,
Bland Holt has some more dramas waiting round
the corner, ready to knock creation when he gives
the signal.
»* * »
Melbourne Bijou, under the direction of
Frank Clark, put forward a hybrid variety show
on Saturday evening. Two stage Irishmen rushed
up to the circular quay at Monte Carlo, in a
gondola, and sang comic songs. The local popu-
lation also sang, and there was something
described as a Blizzard by way of a climax to the
first part. The next part was neither better, nor
much worse, than could be expected, and the
clever gymnast, Ouda, brought proceedings to a
close. A welcome strip of carpet has been laid
down in the Bijou’s noisy corridor, and the public
are tempted by pot-plants and music to go forth
and refresh themselves during the interval, and
the appearance of the corridor on Saturday sug-
gested that the new entertainment was of a
nature to create a strong thirst.
»* * *
At Melb. Opera House the Rickards Co.
flourishes. The infallible Pope, with his colored
colleague, the riotous Sayles, is back again after
a sufficiently long absence. The Sakers, ventrilo-
quist Winton, and numerous other “turns” give
the usual quantity of unparalleled delight. Any
misguided performer who doesn’t give pleasure to
all is promptly suppressed by a wise management.
A new double bill at the little Cambridge
Theatre shows signs of weakness in its hind-
quarters, and the time when this pluckily-con-
ducted establishment will get crowded to the
doors is still in the dim future. In fact,
the Cambridge audiences seem to wax smaller
by degrees. All the same, D’Arcy Stanfield
has hopes of working up a fine business. He
has produced several cheerful comedies in bright
style, and fairly assumes that they ought to serve
as good advts. Arthur Garner is suspected of an
intention to re-open the Alexandra, and the Esty-
Marsh concerts are suffering from neglect. Only
a big name will fill the Town Hall nowadays.
Concerts contain too much walking up and down
stairs and not enough music.
** * *
The Germans of Melb. are preparing for a
grand production of Wagner’s “Lohengrin.”
Either Mr. Laver or Prof. Marshall-Hall will
conduct, and some new singers will make their
appearance. The tenor will be taken by W,
Graupner—brother of the piquant Flo.—who is
said to possess a marvellous tenor voice. The bass
will be Mr. W, Dunlop, who sang so brilliantly
at the Town Hall concert on May 4. “ The Prof-
fessor ” has an immense idea of the vocal powers
of the last-mentioned, and asserts that he has at
last discovered a bass competent to sing Wagner,
for whose music a singer must possess the double
range, bass and baritone. Madame Wiedermann
will sing Elsa’s part. The opera will be produced
either at the Princess’ or the Bijou, for one night
only.

The second annual f£te and tea-fight in aid
of the Distressed Actors’ Fund will be held in
Melbourne before the month is out. The Gaiety
ladies have consented to sell refreshments, button-
holes, &0., on this occasion, and a scheme for turn-
ing their lovely presence to the best possible
account is in course of construction. Every person
who wishes to get a fair look at one or more act-
resses will be supplied with the opportunity to do
so without any attendant risk of being trampled
under foot. It is understood that tickets, entitling
the purchaser to one bun and a smile, will be sold
at the entrance to the tent. Any person lingering
long at the counter will be charged extra or
chucked out. The scheme, which is somewhat as
stated, has been founded on the experience of last
year, when there was a terrible anxiety to get into
each tent, and comparatively little money got out
of it. Apart from the beauty portion of the show,
there will be a bigger, brighter and more varied
entertainment than last year’s. The Distressed
Actors’ Fund is a deserving matter. It is the sole
support of the Dramatic and Musical Association
since the banks busted, and during the past 12
months the fund has dispensed some hundreds of
pounds in the best kind of charity—cash down,
and nothing said about it outside of the board-
room. The chairman of the fund (J. C. William-
son) objects to any publication of the names of
people who receive assistance. That’s good.

Grace Palotta’s trim little figure in “ In Town,” is a joy
to be remembered fondly. She is a rounded, plump,
elegantly-moulded little thing in blue tights and close-
fitting bodice, as perfectly shaped as a sculptor’s ideal
figurette, and as lively on the stage as a dragon-fly in a
sunbeam. There is no resisting the fascination of her
broken English—except by those who think with the
humorless spouse of a Toorak magnate that " the little
gal would be all right if she could speak plain.”
In her dance in “In Town,” Madge Rossell wears a
dress of the color of cigarette-smoke, a blueish grey. It
is entirely appropriate to the dance, a curling, spiral,
cloudy movement, in which the dancer curves her lithe
body in the most gracefully lissome manner, and floats
round the stage like a “light cloud of cigarette smoke
puffed from some wizard’s mouth.”
The dress worn by Maud Hobson, in “ In Town,” is like
nothing in the world but a big bruise. And yet it ia
beautiful in spite of, or perhaps because of, its daring.
The skirt is of blue silk, with a bodice of yellow velvet
with bands of black silk ribbon and black lace. And a
coquettish blue-and-biack hat set jauntily on the masses
of golden fair hair completes a most fetching picture.
Verily the fair Maud, with her diamond-studded black
velvet collar, knows how to show off all her many and
various charms to best advantage.
There is one particular in which the present Gaiety Co.
is immeasurably superior to Lonnen’s. VVe miss the man
in the “ leary ” clothes, covered with black braid and
pearl buttons, who stood in the limelight, and with con-
torted face and voice, inflicted “coster” songs on a
suffering audience. If for nothing else but this, Monk-
house and Co. deserve big houses.

People got a shock at Melb. Royal, the other night.
The top member of the Living Bridge failed to clutch the
opposite cliff, according to custom, and yet the three
bridgers didn’t swing down into the chasm as one would
naturally expect them to do. Curtain was hastily rung
down, but meanwhile the house had clutched the dis-
illusioning fact that there was invisible wire in the case.
