A BEAUTIFUL COMPLEXION.
It has long been known to scientists that for
improving the appearance and beautifying the
face oatmeal is far and away superior to all other
foods, but it had one drawback—the tendency to
heat the blood. To overcome this defect the at-
tention of eminent scientists has been devoted to
the removal of this imperfection ; and now, after
long years of careful and diligent research, art has
triumphed, and Uncle Toby’s Boiled Oats is the
result, for, by skilful manipulation in the manu-
facture, all the heating properties have been ex-
tracted, so that now a beautiful complexion of
peach and ivory may b'e had by anyone who makes
a continuous use of Uncle Toby’s Boiled Oats.
The Wild-Cat Column .
The A.M.P. Society has done well during the
past year, audit would have ( primafaoie) done a good
deal better but for its commendable resolve to provide in
advance for possible losses through the “depression.”
the directors have resolved to set aside £225,000 to cover
anticipated depreciation in Uie value of securities. Tins
makes a total reserve for this purpose of £414,000. Pos-
sibly, it may be found necessary to set aside further
amounts with the same end in view in succeeding years, hut
the matter is one which need trouble policy-holders little.
Despite all deductions, the net profits continue large,
and the A.M.P. still pays—and, to all appearance, will
continue to pay—the largest bonuses in Australia. When
all imaginable depreciation is provided for, and no further
provision is required for this purpose, the bonuses should
grow still larger, and it is always possible that a general
revival may enhance values and make the present reserves
altogether unnecessary. In any case, the directors do
well to make allowance for every contingency. Besides
the above reserves, £14,000 has been written off the value
of office premises, £25,000 set aside to provide for any
future reduction of interest on investments, and £15,001)
for other purposes ; and after all these deductions the
magnificent profit of £431,837 remains to be divided
among policy-holders as the result of the year’s opera-
tions.
The A.M.P.’s premium income for ’94 equals
£1.270,000, and the receipts from interest on investments
£699,000. The total revenue was £28,000 more than
last year, and £480,000 has been added to the accumu-
lated funds, making a grand total of £12,435,240 on hand.
The invested funds yielded £5 11s. lOd. per cent., and
seeing that the society only* estimates a yield of £3 15s.
per cent., the margin is very large indeed. The expenses,
which, in 1892, had reached the apparently irreducible
minimum of 9i per cent, on the total receipts, have now
been reduced to BJ, and apparently a further diminution
may be looked for. In two or three years from now, when
all the necessary reserves have been provided for depre-
ciation, reduction in the estimated rate of interest, £.c.,
the A.M.P. should be able to increase its bonus to a
figure which will break its famous 1892 record. Mean-
while, the results, despite the present transition state
of things, are all that could reasonably he expected.
The A.M.P.’s balance-sheet, which, up to a
year ago, contained about as little real information as
such a document could well give, is now drawn up on
the lines which The Bulletin has suggested with much
reiteration for a good many months past. For the
first lime it shows separately mortgage interest accrued,
mortgage interest overdue and in arrears (these items
seem'hitherto to have been lumped in with the mort-
gages), premiums due but on which the days of grace
have not yet expired, and premiums in arrear. The
result on the whole is satisfactory, and the comparatively
small amount of interest actually in arrear should do
much to dispel certain unpleasant rumors whose existence
was largely owing to the policy of dignified reticence
which the A.M.P.—in common with most other offices—
adopted in these matters.
♦ • • *
Two facts stand out more conspicuously than
the rest among Premier Reid’s financial proposals. One
is that the year 1894 closed with an acknowledged
deficiency of £27l,Bo2—besides another which is not
acknowledged as the first result of Mr. Reid’s financing.
Tim other is that the half-year which ends next month is
likely to close with a deficiency of £240,000, and that Mr.
Reid's alleged surplus of £106,000 is arrived at by about
the most foolish and impudent juggle on record. The
estimated revenue for the half-year is £240,000 less I ban
the expenditure. Therefore, the Premier has delved
among the finances of past years, and discovered certain
items, amounting to £345,700, which his predecessors
paid out of revenue, but which, lie alleges, should have
been paid out of loan moneys instead. Why any amount
should he charged to loans if the Treasurer can pay it out
of revenue passes comprehension, but, without explain-
ing this, Mr. Reid shifts these ancient items into the loan
account and takes £345,700 of loan moneys, which he
puts into the receipts for the current half-year. Also, he
professes to regard this sum as money just received in
the ordii ary way, and this alleged addition will presently
he quoted as an example of how the public income
expands under the blessed influences of Free-raid.
The gall of this device is unique. Mr. Reid’s
predecessors found the money to pay certain liabilities
out of revenue ; the transaction is over and done with,
and Premier Reid simply borrows £345,700 and puts it
into current revenue, and feebly disguises the fact by
saving that it wasn’t he but his pr-decessors who bor-
rowed it. Last year, according to his own figures, the
Reid deficiency was £271,800. It was really £487,000, hut
the Premier charged £216,000 of his own expenditure
to ills predecessor’s accounts in 1893. This half-year
he has a shortage of £240,000, but lie charges that also to
his predecessors, and it is from them also that he gets ids
surplus of £106,000. The grim reality is that Premier
Reid tins gone £727,000 to the bad in 18 months, but has
disguised the fact by annexing revenue from his dead and
gone political ancestors, and by charging expenditure to
those who have gone before him. The deficiency is to be
shuffled offi in the good old way, by borrowing £1,200,000
or thereabouts, and passing the burden on to posterity.
Every N.S.W. financier does the same old, old shuffle, and
the burden is always handed down to that patient ass,
posterity, in the same fashion.
The country now awaits with considerable
interest Mr. Reid’s third financial statement. His first
and second have been characterised by the same bright
and original “ fake,” and as the habit of juggling with
accounts grows upon one, like rum, and opium-eating,
and other bad habits, the next budget will probably be
managed in the same fashion. Probably, in course of
time, the Premier will be found charging current expen-
diture to forgotten Treasurers of 40 or 50 years ago, or
else taking credit as current revenue for money which was
paid away in the time of B igh or Hunter, or some other
of the early fathers of the piovince. Nearly every Trea-
gurer on record lias faked the accounts more or less—
generally more—but G. H. Reid’s mode of faking is
altogether new, so far as The Bulletin is aware.
* * ♦ »
That big and ancient institution, the Bank of
New South Wales, keeps progressing at a time when
most ottier institutions of the kind show a downward
tendency. The gross profit for Uie half-year is £90,260,
against £88,600 for the previous half, and the same divi-
dend of 9 per cent, is declared. The note circulation has
increased by £21,000, and the deposits by £159,000. The
paid-up capital has expanded by £51,000, and the pre-
miums thereon (£l3 882) have gone to swell the reserve-
fund, which now stands at £1,158,000. Coin, bullion, and
cash balances have grown by £850,000, and now reach the
anormous figure of £5,724,000, in addition to £BOO,OOO in
Government and other debentures, and £255,000 in other
realisable securities, making nearly £7,000,000 of imme-
liately available reserves. In every possible respect the
B.N.S.W. shows increased solidity, and its steady pro-
gress, at such a time as the present, is a remarkable proof
of soundness and good management.

The Viqj Insolvency Commission has elicited
that the large sums usually charged to estates by trade
ussignees for "clerical assistance,” “messengers,” and
the like, are simply perquisites of the assignee, and that
tn the great bulk of cases only a fraction of the amounts
to charged are paid for the purposes stated. It is also
Interesting to learn that it is a recognised thing for
assignees to “ whack ” the 5 per cent, commission with
the highly-respectable merchants who put the estate in
the assignee’s way ; but the most damning thing given
in evidence is the allegation that certain firms have occa-
sionally been allowed to prove for much larger sums than
the insolvent really owes, with the result that the little
creditor gets (sa) r ) his ss. in the £ only, but the big one
gets ss. on the actual amount owed, and an additional
fliv. at the same rate on the fictitious debt 1
* * * *
One or two Vic. trade assignees are trem-
bling in their boots as to the result of the evidence
which the Inso'vency Commission now sitting, extracted
(with closed doors) from some half-dozen cf their ex-
empt ov sa.
The Bulletin wants to know why nearly all
the most, startling evidence given before Vic. Insolvency
Commission as to crooked practices of trade assignees has
been omitted from the news reports? Also, why the
interesting items re the lawyers' pickings out of insol-
vent estates are not published. Despite all the proofs
of wholesale plunder by certain trade assignees and their
pals, the lawyers, the former profess no fear as to effectual
remedial legislation resulting. As one of them remarked,
“Trust Uie host of lawyers in Parliament to look after
themselves and us.”

Out of 120 Vic. trade assignees called to furnish
full accounts of their stewardships, 40 odd have failed to
do so, and out of these 40 quite a number have recently
hurriedly cleared out.
* ♦ » •
It is most likely that Vic. trade assignees in the
future will become officers of the Court, and will have to
find security for the amount of the assets in every estate
that falls into their hands. This is sure to wipe out the
very few honest little trade assignees and cause the big
ones to become more bloated than ever.

The Queensland loan of ,£1,250,000 at 3£ per
cent, has realised an average of £lOl 12s. 7d., and “ ex-
penses,” which are a variable quantity, will probably re-
duce it to a little over par. Its purpose is to redeem an
old 6 percent. loan of £765,600, and £500,000 worth of 4
per cent. Treisury bills, both due Ist January, 1896. The
province nominal!)' saves by the conversion £21,586 per
annum in interest, which isn’t much of a feat, for in the
present state of the money-market even the blundering
Treasurer Nelson could hardly avoid making the saving.
Meanwhile, however, the loan has been floated about
nine months before it was needed, so Queensland loses
£32,000 in interest while the money is lying idle. Pre-
sumably it will tie deposited in the Q.N. Bank in the
interim, and when once Queensland public funds get into
the Q.N. Bank they have such a strange and dreadful
tendency to be left there that this circumstance alone is
depressing. Two other things are worth noting : (1) The
Government has already £4,390,000 in that bank, and
as £2,220,000 is said to he “ free,” and the loan to be re-
deemed only amounted to about half that sum, there
seems to be no reason which can bear public inspection
why there should be any loan at all. And (2), in the
general whoop about the “success” of the new loan,
hardly anyone seems to notice how Nelson’s chronic
deficit was first turned into Treasury hills, and how the
Treasury Hills are now being turned into permanent debt,
and tiow the burden is being once more shunted on to our
good old friend, posterity.

A wire from Cargo, N.S.W., says it seems
strange to the people there that so little appears in the
Sydney papers about Cargo mining prospects, and, conse-
quently, the field gets little attention from speculators.
Then \v ( e are told that Gum Flat is looking up; that
fossilised leaves and samples of anthracite coal have been
found in the district, which a long-deceased Govt, geolo-
gist describedojs showing all the indications of enormous
auriferous deposits. That correspondent omits to say
that Cargo has had a heap more money spent on its mines
than they deserved. An English co., the Ironclad, went
down there for many thousands, and a Sydney capitalist
erected at Cargo a few years ago a new 20-head battery,
which only put through one crushing and is now for sale.
It is as easy to find a wild-oat mine at Cargo as at Cool-
gardie.

A steamer, carrying 750 live fat cattl©, will
shortly leave Sydney for England. The shipment of so
many in one bottom will materially clip expenses, which
so far have been outrageous ; though, in spite of all, the
Dangar cattle appear to have netted in England pounds
more than the Sydney market price.

The Bulletin has apprised its readers, from
time to time, of intrigues for the sale and purchase of the
Victorian railways, and now it seems there is a definite
proposal, headed by a well-known newspaper proprietor.

An advt. in a Sydney daily for “a first-class
draper,” last week, elicited 307 applications in one day.
* * * •
A Sydney firm bolding a heavy stock of goods,
on which 15 per cent, duty has been paid, is shipping the
lot to its Melt), branch to get the drawback. The duty on
these goods is to be taken off in N.S.W. on Ist June, and
then the astute importer will ship them back to Sydney
duty free.
* * * *
The once well-known Lucknow mine, Grenfell,
N.S.W., is again in work. This is the property that a
lady, some years ago. referred to in the Vic. Bankruptcy
Court as worth £3,000,000. The party of five miners
has cleaned out 115 ft. of mullock and water and put in
new timber. Work is now in progress at a depth of 315 ft.,
where the reef is 7Jft. wide. They mean to sink 100 ft. in
the quartz in the hopes of getting a new gold-shoot.
• ♦ * •
Another big crash —this time in softgoods.
Operations outside the firm’s business is the cause, result-
ing in the total loss of close upon £200,000 of the princi-
pal’s own money.
» • » *
The Central (8.H.) is developing splendidly.
Besides the white-lead body, which is far more extensive
than was thought, no mine but Block 10 has such a show
of sulphides—not even any similar area of the B.H. Pro-
prietary. The returns of sulphides sent from the Central
to Block 14 hardly give a fair idea of what the mine
can do ; though they are much above the average stipu-
lated for, the best available is not sent.
• * • •
From present appearances no great things need
be expected of the B.H. Junction's patched-up concen-
trators.
• * * *
The Broken Hill mine people are scoring heavily
off the local Labor men. In the lead-poisoning regula-
tions there is a clause prohibiting the employment of lads
under 16 where lead is—that is pretty well everywhere.
The Labor men, getting complaints that the provisions as
to reporting cases of lead-poisoning were not being
adhered to, stirred up the Minister. The managements
reply by putting the regulations in force —especially the
one dealing with employment of hoys. Scores of these
hoys, working on the picking floors and about the shops
and earning good wages, have been dismissed. Result
first, a howl from angry fathers, most of whom themselves
work on the mines and are, or have been, Labor sup-
porters ; and then an inclination to let the regulations
slide. The use of respirators about the furnaces is also
being, for the first time, insisted on ; the men to supply
their own respirators.
* • ♦ •
Acting-Supt. Stewart, of the B.H. Proprietary,
had his first labour trouble last vve’ek. The wheelers at
the Pi. Pirie works knocked off because every three men
were required to do what four had previously done. Mr.
Stewart’s attitude would have satisfied even “Wingen”
Abbott. “There is no strike,” lie said. “Some men say
that they cannot do (lie work; and if they can’t—well,
we shall have to get others.”
♦ • • •
The Central Co.’s purchase of the New Broken
Hill Extended property for “under £1500” (£l3OO odd,
it is understood) is the greatest bargain of the Barrier to
date ; and several mining plants there have gone dirt-
cheap. The timber about the mine must have coat all
the purchase-money ; and the machinery could not he
bought at the foundries for under £5OOO. For two boilers
alone the 00. paid about £l5OO. With this new shaft the
ventilation of the Central may be improved a little —and
not before it is needed.
* » * •
Mr. Howell (B.H. Proprietary) says he will
return to the mine. Some things on the property sup-
port the statement. Howell’s house; for example, instead
of being taken over by Stewart, is being occupied by the
former’s lately-married daughter. Howell, by the way, is
largely interested in some Australian enterprises—the
Menindie Meat Preserving and Boiling Down Works
among the number.

Warden Finnerty has at last administered a
staggen-r to “ friendly ” jumping by ruling that all leases
must be worked by the registered holders while in dis-
pute. If this order is enforced there will he more leases
abandoned within 50 miles of Coolgardie during the next
month than in the whole of Australia.

Something wrong with the White Feather
mine-formerly called M'Aulifle’s. Six months ago it
was regarded as the boss mine of the Feather, and the
best milling show of the whole field. Now said the gold
has disappeared with depth and otherwise. Mine sick.
♦ * ♦ •
A Coolgardie man : “ You are rather rough on
Bayley’s, the only proved mine on the field.” Proved
what? Non-div.-paying ? The Bulletin simply said
what people, ordinarily reliable, told it—that the rich ere
in Bayley’s was exhausted long ago, that nearly all ore in
sight in the mine is poor, and that the thousands of tons
atgrass, alleged many times to be worth ounces to the
ton, may or may not pay crushing exes. In a few days
Bayley’s should strike the reef at the 380 ft. level. If poor
there, the death-knell of the mine is sounded.
♦ * * »
“ Bayley’s Reward Co. contemplates erecting a
cyanide plant to extract the gold lost in the tailings.” It
looks like “ sparring for wind.”

There is still a small amount of rich ore un
touched in the higher levels of Bayley’s Reward. If it
should chance to find its way into the battery at an
opportune time, shares might move up a lot. Several of
the Bayleyites, by the way, made barrels of money out of
the Broken Hill Junction.

■ * * * *
Great Boulder, Coolgardie, 128 tons, 12980 z.
Anticipating men who are writing to The Bulletin to ask
“ What about Coolgardie now?” the writer admits a sur-
prise, but nothing like the surprise it must have been to
the Great Boulder people. This gold, the writer hears, is
not from the reef on which the mine was floated. It was
found in a shaft, and consists of veins running in a
decomposed diorite. It has temporarily saved the mine,
but the man who buys Great Boulders at 255., thinking to
get the money back in divs., is—well, a venturous and
trusting person. , , *
“Better than Coolgardie” is a catch-phrase
often used as a prospectus-heading. It would be more
applicable to the Wentworth mines, out of which over
£‘250,000 worth of gold has come in less than six
months, and over £200,000 of it profit 1 Ex-manager
Collins is arriving in London, and the new manager, Mr.
O. 0. Warnford Locke, is here. Xus Bulletin would like
to bet Locke a hat that he won’t turn out two more ton*
of gold in the time.

Bendigo Coolgardie Co. open to crush for .£2 a

The Lily claim, near White Feather, floated in
London for £90,000 —so say the local papers. The pro-
moters hold out great inducements to the shareholders in
the way of divs. No doubt they will keep their word if
they find a reef containing enough gold.
* * * *
The Londonderry catastrophe was even more
horrible than indicated by the first cable. One end of
the cut or golden bole was already down to the clay seam
or floor where the gold cut out. As soon as it commenced
to rise from the 50ft. cross-cuts the gold vanished there,
too, and only went down a few feet in a short streak.
Had the original owners fired one more shot it would have
cost them a lug part of £180,000! Heaven is sometimes
kind to poor old Australia.

* * * •
Menzies’ Find settlement is on a low, scrubby
flat, with a heavy deposit of fine dust. Is should make a
fruitful fever-bed next summer. The main street con-
tains about 50 offices of wood and canvas, in some of
which the “best brands” of neck-lubrication are con-
stantly in stock. The cost of living is £1 15s. per week,
and wages-men get from £3 10s. to £4.
♦ ♦ ♦ ♦
Potatoes and onions fetch Is. a pound at Men
zies’. Hamper and “ tinned dog” make the usual meal.
• * • •
Re Fermoy (Q.) opal mines. One buyer in
March last bought £lOOO worth of opal. Men sink down
15ft. until they get into what is called “pipe opal”—a
valuable kind which can be worked up into bars. The
country is, however, very patchy.

» * » *
The Central Queensland Gold-Mining Proprie-
tary, possessing about 1300 acres of freehold and lease-
hold, including a number of quartz-reefs, ironstone lodes,
and some more or less rich alluvial ground, has
determined to raise working capital in England.
The trustees have secured independent reports
throng!) the Port Curtis Chamber of Commerce. Q.,
supplemented with a certificate from the A.J.S. Bank
showing the amount of alluvial gold already taken out.
The latter totals up to the not insignificant figure of
28h.0000z., and it is alleged that a scheme of hydraulic
sluicing, gold-dredging, and quartz-crushing-involving
an outlay of £60,000 for machinery—on hulk stuff averag-
ing a pennyweight or two to the load, will return some-
thing like £6OOO per week —certainly a big order. Those
who get in on the ground floor of the original Proprietary
shares (£5) should do well enough.
» * * »
The mammoth providoring establishment at the
Havmarket, Sydney, is about to add another to its many
departments-a gentlemen’s hair-dressing saloon, which
will start with a complement of about 20 hands.
* * • *
Melbourne’s biggest universal providers employ
1066 hands, to be reinforced after additions to premises
now in progress. Despite the depression, last year’s turn-
over was the biggest on record. The concern has dealt
out death and destruction to the smaller traders.
* ♦ ♦ •
The Bulletin’s London correspondent by last
mail: ‘‘Londonderry’s, which were faked to a premium of
10s. to 15s. on the strength of some supposed Paris buy-
ing, suddenly slumped to 14s. in one act. On the reports
of the French deal speculators bought, hoping to unload
at the expense of the unsophisticated foreigner, but they
found themselves landed, as Mr. Myring’s deal was solely
for the sale of vendors’ shares, of which there are more
than a couple of hundred thousand to be got rid of. So it
will always be until the ’Derry is proved by results to be
something more than a mere pocket. Whenever the
promoters and underwriters try to get out, down go the
shares —and quite right, too, for a more barefaced grab
never was known. Had North, Pingall and Myringbeen
contented with a reasonable profit, and made the capital
even £400,000, the shares would now be marketable at a
good premium. As it is, the mine hangs round the neck
of the market like an old man of the sea. It is rumored
that the ’Derry has collapsed.”

A well-known Melb. solicitor lately in-
sured his life for .£50,000.
Cosmo Newbery accomplished the impos-
sible. Recovery £j;om, or with, a fractured spine.
David Mitchell, Melba’s father, has in-
creased his holdings of Vic. land since 1882 by
20,547 acres.
Three of the original Mt. Morgan syndi-
cate have already been smitten with paralysis, a
fate which Ross, of Taranganba, shares with
them.
The objectionable system of Judge’s rela-
tives practising before the old man in Sydney has
now descended to the city and suburban police-
courts.
George Dean is a Freemason. Ordinary
men may be hanged, but all the courts in Aus-
tralasia couldn’t hang a Freemason unless the
lodges especially wanted him hanged.

Dr. J. ASHBURTON THOMPSON, of
Sydney.
Matthew Davies is getting an occasional
s’ice of Melb. Divorce Court business. Last week
he appeared for a co-respondent in a case where
the petition was dismissed with costs.
Rose, M.P., who thrust his knife into
Sydney’s big brewers last week, was a Parramatta
draper for many years, and picked up his peculiar
style o? speaking in the Burwood Debating Club.
There are probably only two men in South
Australia who sign themselves “J. Langdon.”
Oae is editor Bonython, the other Parsons ; one
the defendant, the other the plaintiff in the S.A.
libel-action.
Cardinal Moran and Arthur Galton, both
practically new-chums, regard themselves as
most competent of authorities on early Australian
history, each being especially eulogistic of the
other’s fitness for writing in that field.
Henry Gyles Turner, manager of the
Commercial Bank of Australia, is getting on
with his “History of Australia.” He has a
remarkable library of Australian books, the collec-
tion of which has been a hobby with him for years.

Sydney Daily Telegraph credits Rev.
Haweis as being the pioneer champion of Pontius
Pilate !—whereas he is, of course, about the ten-
thousand-and-oneth. Mr. Haweis mostly runs in
well-trodden tracks, but, wearing pointed high
heels, he leaves new impressions.
“ Honor thy father.” This is used as an
excuse for sons and sons-in-law practising before
“the old man” in Australian courts. By the
way, a solicitor who appeared before his father, a
N.S. Wales magistrate, the other day, amused
the court by inadvertently blurting out : “But,
pa, that’s not in the statute !”
The defaulting law-clerk, who was last
week sentenced to three years’ imprisonment, left
Melbourne under the name of “ Dudley Brookes.”
Even the danger of his position could not induce
him to choose a common, unostentatious cognomen
like Smith or Brown. There are some people who
would rather be dead than ordinary.
An old native of the Hawkesbury, Mr.
W. T. Price, died recently at Richmond. Mrs.
Price thus becomes a widow for the fourth time.
Her third husband was Mr. Benjamin Isaacs, per-
haps the first printer to take a press and plant
over the Blue Mountains, where he conducted for
years the Bathurst Advocate in the forties.
Whatever professedly denominational
newspapers may do, the Australian Star should
give over loading its “ leader ” columns with such
expressions as “the Lord Bishop of Goulburn.”
There is no such Australian title; there is no
such person as the title would indicate ; and, as
poor Don Lane used to say, “ There’s no such
thing.”
A Protectionist member of N.S.W. Assem-
bly, whose “ effusions ” have appeared regularly
for years past in the Protectionist organ of his
district, is compiling a volume of his versicles for
book publication. Most of the pieces begin with
“Oh, dear me!” or “ Oh, my!” and if not care-
fully edited will, it is rumored, go far to kill Pro-
tection in Australia.
The Bulletin this week prints the por-
trait of Dr. J. Ashburton Thompson, of Sydney,
who has been awarded a 50-guinea prize by the
committee of the Father Damien Memorial Fund
in recognition of his leprosy-researches. Dr.
Thompson is the chief executive officer of the
N.S.W. Health Department, one of the most
enlightened, energetic, and best-managed Aus-
tralian bureaux.
Poor old Q.C., M.L.C. Solomon, of Suva’
Fiji, will be greatly missed. A peculiarity of his
was to tell a story to anybody he could buttonhole.
He used to repeat the same old yarns over and
oyer, and if the unwilling listener protested or
hinted that he fancied he had heard him tell it
before, the old chap would say “Did I? Well,
I’ll tell it you again for practice.”
Recently died at Westwood, near Rock-
hampton, Wm. Kelman, one of the best-known
of C.Q. pioneers. In 1854 Kelman took up
country on the Dawson and the Isaacs; afterwards
founded Meteor Downs station (near Springsure)
and Glenormiston, on the Queensland-South Aus-
tralian border. Went down in the financial
troubles of ’B6 and ’94, and, game to the last, left
for Ooolgardie, where illness and poverty broke
him down. Returned lately to C.Q. and died at
Westwood —a poor man, who, thirty years ago,
could ride 150 miles in a straight line through his
own territory.

MADGE ROSSELL.
A S.A. Wesleyan parson holds a unique
record. Five years ago he, then a parson and
temperance lecturer, made a fortune in Broken
Hills and left “the vineyard.” He invested much
money in a brewery, but failed, and was for awhile
a clerk to the eo. that bought the business. Re-
cently he was re-admitted to the church, and he is
now a humble divine up Ooolgardie way.
Henry Gyles Turner, appearing as a wit-
ness in the Farrar case at Melbourne Insolvency
Court, was asked by Purves, Q.C., if he had ever
told a lie intentionally. Turner replied, “Not of
late years.” This ought to be tidings of comfort
and joy for Commercial Bank depositors. Also,
the bank ought to announce itself as under the
management of George Washington Redivivus,
Mr. Justice Simpson, the last-appointed
N.S.W. Supreme Court Judge, has, it is said,
signified that he does not wish his son to practise
before him. The other Judges might well follow
the example of their youngest brother, and so
avoid the rude, but effective, interference of the
Legislature. Apropos, the late Mr. Justice Moles-
worth (Vic.) virtually declined to let his son prac-
tise in his Court.
The hand-shaking sustained by Gov. Hope-
toun in good-byeing the Melbourneites resulted in
a bad time for H. E. all the way to Colombo.
Through the mad squeeze of the crowd, a ring on
the gubernatorial little finger was so bent and
pressed into the flesh that it was with difficulty
removed, and then not until it had caused a
nasty laceration which, all through the voyage,
remained greatly inflamed.
Cosmo Newbery, the Melb. analyst who
died the other day, in his time was an expert wit-
ness in many most notable criminal cases. One
was that of Reynolds, the Queen-st. (Melb.)
engineer, who found an infernal machine awaiting
him at his office one morning and was killed by
the explosion. An ex-member of the Prince
Edward Island Parliament was tried for the
murder of Reynolds; but acquitted.
Judge Harding is continually astonishing
the Bananalanders by his eccentricities. The
other day, at Rockhampton, he told a poor o'd
deaf prisoner named Spencer that his deafness
was assumed, and accused the unfortunate man,
who suffers from a weakness in the facial nerves,
of making grimaces at the court. The jury showed
their opinion of the Judge by bringing in a ver-
dict in direct opposition to his Honor’s summing-
up.
Hone Heke, M.H.R. (M.L.), has at his
native place, Kaikohe, Bay of Islands, a suit of
armour presented to his great grand-uncle by
King William IV. “ for services rendered to Capt.
Sadler, of H.M.S. Buffalo,” in 1833. With the
armour was a letter from the Earl of Aberdeen
(shown to the writer by Hone Heke), in which he
says: “The King, my master, desires that you
will accept a suit of armour, such as was worn in
former days by his warriors, but which now
only used by his own body-guard.” The letter is
dated from the Colonial Office, London, Feb., ’35.
The White Knight will be effluxed by
time from his lofty billet as Chairman of
Melb. Board of Works on the last of this month,
and if re-elected (which is almost certain) must
grub along on £l5OO a year and his savings. Mr.
Fitzgibbon says that had not a clause been in-
serted in the Retrenchment Act docking the
Chairman £5OO on termination of the current
four years’ office, he (Fitz.) would, of his own
accord, have returned to the Board the same per-
centage of his salary as that by which the highest
of the salaries of its officers was reduced—viz.,
15%. The statement comes a trifle late. The
self-sacrificial intention does not seem to have
been mentioned before Parliament clinched the
matter.
Gilbert Parker is to marry Miss Vantine,
the handsome daughter of a wealthy New York
importer. Soon after Gilbert first burst upon
Sydney as the Rev.
Horatio Gilbert Par-
ker, ho was credited
with attempting a
very lofty matri-
monial flight, which,
however, didn’t come
off.
John Hancock, the burly Trades Hall
leader in Victoria, is 50 this year, and also cele-
brated his silver-wedding in August. He is
physically the biggest man in the Yic. Parlia-
ment, and he had the largest number of votes cast
for him at the last general election. The Argus
has taken to praising him lately for his “ humor
and good nature.” If any of the Trades Hall
people read the Argus this would be enough to
make Hancock distressed.
The egg-bald barrister, Coldhain (Vic.),
when opposing Premier Turner at last general
election in the Freetrade interest, had to stand a
deal of chaff. On one occasion, when question-
time arrived, a hairy individual in the back asked,
“Is the candidate in favor of a tax on bald ’eads?”
“That,” replied Ooldham, “is the first sensible
question asked to-night. There is a tax of 25 per
cent, on human hair, and yet (pointing to his
head) we cannot produce it locally.”
When Major-General Hutton reviewed the
Vic. troops at Langwarren encampment, he issued
a report as to his opinion of the manoeuvres. In
the course of it he devoted a paragraph to praising
one particular officer—Capt Reay, of the Mounted
Rifles. Both Argos and Age printed the whole-
report, with the exception of this particular para-
graph, which they both cut out. Now, Reay is
the editor of the Herald. Such are the little-
nesses of which the “great papers ” are capable
Photo. by Johnstone, O’Shannessy & Co., Melb.
C. EGERBERG BORCHGREVINK, Ant-
arctic Explorer and Lecturer .
Mr. Carsten Egeberg Borchgrevink, the scien-
tist and South Polar explorer, was the first man
to step on to the mainland of Terra Incognita. He
has been in Australia for about eight years, and,
while in Q. Survey Dept, gained considerable,
reputation through scaling Mt. Lindsay, aprecipi
tons peak near the border. Several skilled moun-
taineers had previously failed to perform this feat,
and there are several tales of mysterious disap--
pearances of a more than legendary character.
Four years ago he came to Sydney and joined the,
scientific branch of the Forest Dept. He wa.c
afterwards science master and teacher of modern,,
languages at Cooerwull Academy. As soon, how-
ever, as he ascertained that the Antarctic was in*
Melbourne bound South, he threw up his engage--
ment and took the only position available, that,
of man before the mast. Mr. Borchgrevink is a*
native of Norway, but his mother was an English,-
woman. He was educated at Gjertsen College*.
Christiania, and afterwards studied science in
Saxony. His brother has been, for some 10 years,
representative of Norway and Sweden in Egypt,
where he occupies the position of Procurator-
General of the Foreign Tribunals.
Bishop Murray
(R.C.), of Maitland, re-
ported as dying, was
formerly private secretary
to Card. Cullen at Dub-
lin, and when he was sent
to Maitland was suc-
ceeded in the post by Dr.
(now Cardinal) Moran.
The Cardinal and Bishop
Murray first met at Rome
when one was 12 and the
other 14 years of age. It
was Dr. Murray who got
Dr. Moran “translated”
to Sydney in ’B4.
A fine sportsman
was the old- time (Q.)
Judge Blakeney. They
were holding sports in a,
paddock adjoining the-
court-house of a Westeiin
town while the ancient,
was trying a man
slaughter case. His son,,
who was travelling cir-
cuit with him, was a con-
testant in the high jump;,
which could be seen
through the open win-
dows of the court. The
Judge, who was watch-
ing the sport, forgot all
a' mt the legal business
in hand. “Go it, Mickey,
my son,” he kept saying,
“at him ! at him ! Well
leapt, me boy ! Well
leapt!” Once there was
a dog-fight in the court
during a case. A police-
man interfered. “Lave
’em alone,” yelled the
ould sport. “Let ’em have it out!” and out
they had it.

gaol for three months for stealing two pairs of
trousers. It was the poor woman’s misfortune to
be single.

noble Premier has fallen in love. That would ac-
count for a good deal of his insomnia.

It has arrived ! Long ago The Bulletin
predicted that Parkes would one day lead the
N.B-W. Protectionists.
“ Were it not for the work necessitated
by retrenchment half the Service could be dis-
pensed with.” —Ftc. Pub. servant.
A careful estimate, made by a man who
knows, shows that not more than 25 men of the
present N.S.W. Assembly could sit there without
payment of members.
The Mount Alexander Mail has changed
hands quietly. Judging from recent issues it
would seem that some very good friend of Ex-
Premier Patterson has now a finger in the pie,
Melb. Age has a mild, almost appreciative,
leader on Premier Reid’s proposal to enter
on a course of Freetrade. Under such a system,
the Vic. manufacturers would need no Federation.
London Sunday Times reports joyfully
that the Reid tariff “opens up a new market ” for
England. N.S. Wales was established as a dust-
heap for England. It is still to be a dust-heap
for England.
The license of speech allowed M.P.’s
makes reasonable legislative progress impossible.
Maoriland has adopted a time-scale which seems
to have lived down all objections. Why don’t the
mainland provinces follow suit?
Three hundred Hovas “slaughtered” by
the French—so runs the cable. But the cable
never says that 300 Zulus have been “slaughtered ”
by the English. It is always : “ The enemy, who
fought desperately, 10-t 300 men.”
In the opinion, and no doubt the hope, of ex-
Minister W. J. Lyne, next general election will
completely, and for a generation at least, extin-
guish the Labor-party as an influence in N.S. W,
Parliament. But it won’t come off.
A M.P. last week referred to N.S.W.
Justice-Minister Gould as “ a jelly-fish.” But the
only fish that the bowelless dispenser of nickel-
plated gags really resembles is the hard, dry, salt,
bloodless “Bombay duck” that you eat with curry.
On the “ temporary ” staff of one N.S. W.
department there is an officer plurally important,
because his kith and kin have great political'in-
fluence : he has been three times “retrenched ”
publicly, and three times taken back again in some
underground fashion,
N.S.W. Premier Reid’s “clarion cries”
about Australian Federation are discounted by
*tl,e fact that Victoria already regards his Budget
proposals as strangling Federation, inasmuch as it
absolutely scares off that province from any
attempt at making a similar tariff.
Numbers of Japs, have lately arrived in
the Bundaborg district from the North. Upon
whom the local planters, sniffing cheap labor,
have cast greedy eyes, to be repelled with the cool
remark: “We are not going to work for any
boss : we are going to rent land and work it for
ourselves,”
One M‘Lean, M.L.A., writes to the Dean
defence committee that he is averse to promoting
agitation against “the calm, deliberate decisions
of the courts of justice,” and sends to the papers
an exact copy of his precious screed. Calm and
deliberate justice ! Mount Rennie calm and
deliberate justice !
S.M. Herald, posedly and supposedly a
Freetrade journal, deliberately advocates the un-
flinching continuance of a N.S.W. railway policy
of Protection. Only the Herald doesn’t call it
“ protection,” but merely a system of “defensive
measures.” Verily, the Sydney morning dailies
are the Aholah and Aholibah of N.S.W. Free-
trade !
A Sydney civil-servant has just taken re-
trenchment by the forelock. The postmaster of a
branch office (screw, .£300) recently retired after
long service. His assistant (screw, .£200) offered
to fill the vacancy without increase of salary, and
his offer has been accepted. A noxious example.
There are a thousand people ready to take his
billet by Dutch auction.
Prendergast, the Vic. Labor M.P., petu-
lantly says he will not attend any meeting or speak
in any room where H. H. Champion is present.
Suppose that Champion gets elected for Barba-
ment at next opportunity, as is not at all unlikely,
it will be interesting to see whether Prendergast
insists upon sacrificing the <£3oo a year rather
than sit in the House with Champion.
When George Ash, M.P. (S.A.), Premier
Kingston’s law-partner, was ruined by a libel-
action, he vowed he would see the libel laws
altered. He made law-reform his battle-cry, and
headed the poll. There has been no reform ; but
Ash, now a lawyer, is found in ’95 as prosecuting
counsel pressing against a journalist that very law
which worked his o»m ruin in ’B9 ! This is a
peculiar world.
Both in N.S. Wales and Victoria many of
the so-called telegraph messenger boys are married
men of mature years, with families. Victoria is
shutting down on the old and sedate married boy
as being too expensive and ridiculous for his work,
and N.S. Wales will some day have to do like-
wise. If it doesn’t, some of the boys will by-and-
bye arrive at the statutory ago of 60, and require
to be superannuated.
Victorian: “Premier Turner's dabbling
in business jobs is a downright scandal. The
other day, in CoJlingwood court, Lyons, Turner’s
partner, protested with excellent grounds against
an apparent packing of the Bench ; whereupon
Gaunson, on the other side, made a remark which
might have been expected. He said that Lyons
could, of course, make observations which nobody
else dared to. Quite unwarrantable, but why
should the handle be given ?”
Pendleton’s appointment as S.A. Railway
Commissioner may wreck the Kingston Ministry.
They have wrangled over the appointment for
months, and then selected Traffic-manager Pen-
dleton as a compromise, the Cabinet being divided
as to the merits of Smith and Hill, who, with
Neill, are the retiring Commissioners. The public
regard Smith as the man who should have been
appointed. About .£5OOO a year will be directly
saved by the new appointment.
B. R. Wise fixed a stony eye on “the
natural leader of the N.S.W. Freetrade party ” the
other day. Parkes’words were : “ The great states-
men of England never attempted to reduce the
tariff to tobacco, spirits and opium. It is the
device of shallow persons in all walks of life to
do something which sensible men would never
dream of.” But that is just the tariff—stimulants
and narcotics —which Wise has, in season and out
of season, promulgated and defended. Does “ the
natural leader ” think he is now strong enough to
do without his volatile but faithful henchman?
The Argus fiercely opposed the efforts of
Davitt’s admirers to secure Melb. Town Hall for
the purpose of a demonstration of welcome. It
asks :
Is not the real purpose of Mr. Davitt’s visit, like the
visits of the Redmonds and Mr. Dillon and Sir Thomas
Esmond, to work up feeling in Australia for the political
and party cause of Home Rule on the other side of the
world ?
The Argus is satisfied that this is the purpose of
Davitt’s coming, and concludes that no concessions
whatever should be made. If May and George of
York decide to visit Australia it is to be hoped the
Argus will continue to argue on these lines.
From the time Parkes first handled a port-
folio till to-day he has, while constantly arguing
that the success of the present system of constitu-
tional politics demands the presence of two dis-
tinct parties, separated by divergent conscientious
convictions, held himself on hand for a coalition
with any enemy whose assistance he valu-
able in the pursuit of power. He allied himself
with James Martin in hostility to John Robert-
son, and afterwards led a bitter attack on both.
Later he coalesced with all and sundry to destroy
Robertson, and still later coalesced with Robert-
son against all and sundry. To-day the coalition
with Dibbs aims at the destruction of Reid ; to-
morrow, for the leopard cannot change its spots,
Parkes will stand ready to coalesce with Reid to
destroy Dibbs.

Sydney Telegraph, which supports the
Reid (Jovt., publishes telegrams alleged to bo
received from nearly every town in N.S. Wales,
stating that the population is jubilant over the
Reid budget. The Herald, which doesn’t sup-
port Reid nearly so enthusiastically, has seemingly
received hardly any such telegrams, and doesn’t
know anything at all about the said enthusiasm,
that the budget has been received with horror and
disgust. ’Twas ever thus.
THE LADY-FOOTRALL MOVEMENT.—THE AUSTRA-
LIAN CAPTAINESS GETS READY.
A London weekly sniggers at the sweet
ingenuousness of Lord Hopetoun in promising to
accept the billet of Gov.-General of Australia as
“a sacred duty ” as soon as the federated provinces
are ready for him. Poor Hopey’s little head was
much swelled when he fired off his farewell (MS.)
speech in Melbourne. The Cockney journal
reminds him that a good many noble lords would
feel it their sacred duty to put in tenders for the
Australian G.G- job.
No Australian
politician can do juf-
tice to his trust, nor
to himself, if he omit
to post himself in the
details of Queensland,
Maoriland and Vic-
torian constitutional
crises. It can never
be known when an-
other conflict, to be
waged on precisely
the same terms, may
be precipitated upon
any province. If Vic.
toria is sincere upon
the great principle of
one person one vote,
a fray may be near
at hand. Should all
constitutional means
fail, the local Tory
gang will not scruple,
if there is the smallest
loophole, to drive the
democratic party into
thedistressfuldangers
of a deadlock.
The abortive
motion by Stephens,
M.P., in N.S.W.
Assembly, to confer
the franchise on men,
women and children,
was not the unargu-
able proposal the
House considered it.
Belgium actually
gives a married man
two votes at Parlia-
mentary elections, and
the tendency of public
thought there is _in
the direction of giving
a man with children
more influence in the
State than one with-
out. Had the mover,
instead of presenting
himself as an original
thinker, mentioned
the source of his in-
spiration, perhaps he
would not have been laughed out of the House.
Premier Turner’s Retrenchment Commit-
tees have ■ unearthed several public-service in-
formers, who have secret interviews with the
members and tell tales about the convivial,
socialistic, and other proclivities of their office
associates. One of these unsavoury creatures the
other day reported a colleague for having in con-
versation said that he “ didn’t care a damn for
members or Ministers.” The Premier has been
foolish enough to take serious notice of the com-
plaint, and a formal enquiry has been held.
N. S. W. Premier Reid took dancing-lessons
at a fashionable Sydney academy a few years ago.
Ladies who attended the class along with him still
speak kindly of him as a good-hearted person who
only trod on their feet by accident. G. Porgey
never made much progress on the light, dis-
tracted toe; he was a heavy and slow-moving
dancer, and many a snow-white foot was filled
with anguish when the statesman came down on
it in his plain, business-like fashion. Put George
R. always apologised in such a heavenly fashion
that no one could really feel vindictive. When he
left it was generally believed that he knew less of
dancing than when he first came, but most of the
other jpupUs wero aqrry to_lose him all the same*
Maoriland proves the reality of its surplus
by transferring <£150,000 of visible cash to the
Public Works Fund. It is a long time since any
Australian surplus was sufficiently visible to be
transferred anywhere, except by an inky process
in a ledger.
Nothing could more
etrongly display the inepti-
tude of Melb. City Corpora-
tion than the fact that the
weak and pig-headed Snow-
den should have been thrice
elected Mayor. He has not
an idea beyond grovelling at
the feet of a Governor or
pushing forward a sweating
policy for the Corporation
employes. He refused per-
mission to the May Day
procession to march through
the streets, and could not
bottle up his petty feelings
against popular movements
when it was asked that the
Town Hall might be made
available for the reception of
Davitt. “Capture the muni-
cipalities ” ought to be the
cry of the Melb. Radicals
for the next few yea'rs. The
various municipalities are
little hot-beds of Fat-man
tyranny and class greed;
and the City Corporation is
the very worst of the lot,
A visit to Melb. Trades
Hall and Friday evening will
vividly bring home themani-
fest truth of a recent Bul-
letin article : “ Men with
Mouths and Men with Ears.”
All the tin gods of the hall,
Hancock, Prendergast, Bar-
rett, &c., possess fog-horn
voices, and yell in the Council
chamber as if they were ad-
dressing multitudes of 50,000
in the open air. The few men
there who are gifted with a
superior perception and a
logical faculty of expression
are weak-voiced tenors, and,
therefore, only serve as ob-
jects of Hancock’s primitive
humor and Prendergast’ssar-
casms. It would be centuries
before one of these could
command the attention of
any Labor League sufficiently
to gain its selection, while
the average bull-toned new-
chum agitator finds himself in
Parliament within 36months.
A N.S.W. land-tax of Id. in the £ would
extract amounts as follows : From 11,000 holdings,
40s. or under; from 2800 holdings, from £2 to
.£6; 7400 holdings, from <£6 to <£18; 5800 hold-
ings, from <£lB to <£4o; from 1600 holdings, over
.£4O.
Government by Contradiction asserts itself
again in N.S. Wales. The Reid Budget, which
the country is asked to swallow, consists partly
of good beef and’partly of leprosy-scales; of pro-
mises of honest administration on one hand,
and of increasing doses of foreign-trade on the
other. If you won’t .swallow the leprosy-scales
you can’t have the beef, and that’s all about it.
But when you look at the crowd who would be
associated in pulling Reid down what do you see?
The hoary, self-sufficient Parkes, a thousand
times traitor to democracy ; the empty braggart
Dibbs, teetotum tool of craftier heads ; the
See-pbts-Garvan crowd; the envious Ringnose
Wise; and Epps astride of the National Ass. It
is a pleasant dilemma.

Mrs Currell, of South Melbourne,
identified the mysterious head ae that of
her husband because she believed she saw
on it a wen, a broken nose and a decayed,
tooth, all of winch existed on her hus-
band. This morning Dr Neild found Mrs
Currell was entirely mistaken. None of
the marks described wore on the derelict
head.

THAT TREADMILL —OR ON THE WAY TO FEDERATION.
“ Our reporter visited G. Dibbs at Emu Plains on Saturday afternoon, in the hope of eliciting some further information about the attitude of himself and other members of his party tow ards the contemplated alliance with H. Parkes. I'11 tell you what, said he, with a
twinkle in his eye, ‘you see that framework there— the thing with the rollers underneath ? . Well, that’s a treadmill. You fasten the horse in the box, where he cannot stand still. To retain his footing he is compelled to keep the machinery in motion .' "—Sydney D.T.

Why, in the name of Honesty, should the
liberation of Dean, the nominally-convicted wife-
poisoner, be dependent on the production of con-
vincing new evidence in his behalf? The public
agitation in his favor was not based on the dis-
covery of new evidence by the defence, but on the
fact that the Crown ease was, on its merits, not
good enough to drown a kitten on ; that the ver-
dict was based on the preposterously ignorant and
ridiculous medical theory propounded by the
Attorney-General and accepted (as per His Honor’s
notes) by the allegedly learned Judge—on the un-
pardonable judicial apology for the self-admittedly
vile career of the chief witness for the prosecution,
and on the judicial disparagement of the prisoner’s
undoubtedly good past character—on the judicial
brow-boating of a divided jury, and the insidious
and indefensible judicial suggestion that a verdict
of guilty could be accompanied by a recommenda-
tion to mercy.
Let the public keep their eyes steadily on the
fact that it is the Crown ease, not Dean’s, which
is on its trial. A hundred thousand voices declare
that Dean’s conviction was a bogus conviction
that the Crown case was not proved, and that so
far as the evidence produced at the trial for the
prosecution is concerned Dean ought to be a free
man. Yet here we have the “lawnorder” con-
tingent, headed by the ever - sneaking S.M,
Herald, organ of judicial absolutism, declaring
that the release of Dean should be contingent,
not on the Crown’s proving his guilt, but on the
prisoner’s proving his innocence !
• • *
J udge Windeyer’s remark, when sentencing
George Dean to death, that he was as sure that he
poisoned his wife as if he (Judge Windeyer) had
seen him do it, is but a repetition of what His
Honor said 11 years ago, when sentencing the
Frenchman Cordini to death, at Deniliquin, for
the murder of the hawker Mison. J udge Windeyer
told Cordini that he was as certain that he mur-
dered Mison as if he had seen him do it;
Cordini was duly hanged. The evidence was
wholly circumstantial, and, strangely enough, the
same reasons as those raised in Dean’s case—the
unfairness of the trial—were put forward as grounds
for a reprieve. A number of people at Deniliquin
still think that Cordini’s innocence—which he pro-
tested even on the scaffold—will yet be established.
# * •
“ I don’t know,” said a briefless wigman,
the other day, during a discussion of the George
Dean case, “ that a better than Rogers could be
secured to head the Commission—unless, indeed,
a layman acquainted with the orderly taking
of evidence could be secured. Salomons wouldn’t
do. Windeyer once insulted him grievously.
Pilcher, though strong, is still dependent on the
courts. It’s this way : If a vacancy be created on
the Bench and the release of Dean means Win-
deyer’s retirement—who is to fill it ? Want won’t.
If, then, Salomons, Stephen, Pilcher, or any other
in the front rank, killed (in a legal sense, of course)
Windeyer, it would be indecent if the killer took
hi t place. And the same indecency would lie if a
District Court Judge acted as chairman and
claimed, as all of them are at present claiming,
promotion from the District to the Supreme Court
Bench. Rogers’s position in N.S.W. legal affairs
is unique; George Dean's case is unique; and
unless the whole thing were to be kept in the
hands of laymen the very best man has been
picked.”
* # *
Shifting Judges is not such an easy thing
to do as some people think. Thirty years ago one
Benjamin Boothby was second Judge of the
Supreme Court in South Australia. He made
things lively there by denying the validity of
sundry Acts of the local Parliament, especially
one or two bearing on Torrens’ Real Property
Act, which, qf course, was very strongly opposed
to the “vested interests” of lawyers. Also, he
was in the habit of creating mild excitement by
refusing to recognise the Attorney-General, and
later after the retirement of Chief Justice
Cooper—by loudly and officially expressing the
opinion that his successor, Richard Hanson, was
not really a Judge. Then he inquired into the
status of Gwynne, the third Judge, with the
eminently satisfactory result (to himself) that the
only legally-appointed Judge in the province was
Benjamin Boothby, who was in the happy con-
dition of holding letters patent from the Crown.
Things got a bit mixed in South Australia, and
people began to think and speak scornfully about
the administration of justice. Parliament sailed
in, and both Houses passed resolutions asking for
his removal, but it wasn’t until this had been done
twice that Benjamin’s mess was cooked. Austra-
lian Ministries aren’t in the same street with
Australian Judges, when it comes to a question
of moving out.
# • #
The way in which the court-martial in the
Ringarooma affair calmly tried the wrong issue
was really very pretty, and very effective, too.
The real question was, whether Surgeon Lea was
justified in acting in a certain way. The question
which the court insisted on trying was, whether
the accused did or did not act in that way. As
the fact that he did so act was never disputed,
the court of captains, by solemnly and cere-
moniously trying this question, at once settled all
doubt as to the result, and avoided all possibility
of hurting the feelings of their prosecuting
brother-captain.
* * *
Dear Bulletin, — Queer how few pro-
testors against the Ringarooma court-martial
decision could keep the “British fair-play”
wheeze out of the discussion of an affair which
proves that there isn’t any such thing. The
average Briton is pathetically blind to the fact
that, while some of the nations look upon him as
merely a pragmatical ass, the majority regard him
as a judicious blend of filibuster and snufilebustcr.
It will take the Englishman 10,000 years to per-
ceive that his complacent impartiality is one of
the standing-jokes of that unimportant portion of
the world outside the British Islands. —Gosmop.

* * #
Two courts-martial in less than five months
seem to show that the “Australian” cruiser,
Ringarooma, is a little floating hell. The first
court dismissed the Navigatiug-Lieutenant from
the ship because he broke down after being
almost continuously on duty for 12 days,” and
let off the Captain, who had forced or allowed his
sleepy but sleepless subordinate to navigate the
smp through a maze of coral reefs. The second
has dismissed the ship’s surgeon from the service
for “ behaving with disrespect ” to the Captain—a
sufficiently vague charge in any case. Although
Lieut. Chetwynd was dismissed the Ringarooma,
it is satisfactory to know that the Imperial autho-
rities have since appointed him to a similar posi-
tion on the China station.
• • •
One day last week a cold-blooded cable-
gram chronicled the plain, unvarnished fact that
the Earl and Countess of Hopetoun had arrived in
London. No guard of honor, no cannons, no
excited reporters asking them if they couldn’t
think of something they had forgotten to saj' to
the “people of Victoria,” no pot-plants at the
railway-station, no unmistakeable signs of emotion
—nothing. They simply arrived and drove off in
a cab, or barouche, that was waiting for them.
Vulgar draymen cursed their driver for a blanky
hamatoor, who didn’t know where he wasa-gittin’
to, urchins clambered on to the step and thrust
newspapers under the Countess’s mended nose,
cockney clerks stared superciliously at them from
the knife-boards of ’buses, and the noble pair felt
inclined to weep. They did, indeed. There’s no
doubt that lumps came into their throats when
they considered the awful difference between
departing from Melbourne and arriving in London.
Hopetoun is still the Governor of Victoria (on
half-pay)—but the vanity of vice-regal greatness
has revealed itself to him and his good lady under
very painful circumstances. He doesn’t yet know
himself for what he is ; he still hangs on to the
slippery tail-end of the idea that Australia feels
lonely without him, but—our colonial oath ! Hopey
soon found out that he wasn’t what he had been
seven weeks before.

Melb. Hekald has excited a preliminary
thrill of interest in dear Governor Brassey, that is
to be, by publishing his coat-of-arms, crest, &c.
They make a weird, peculiar picture. There’s the
king of clubs, or some similar potentate, with a
small gander roosting on his crown, and a larger
bird of the same breed supporting him on either
side. A crusader’s shield plays the part of a
heraldic poultice to the king’s oval carcase, like-
wise the big ganders carry shields suspended from
dog-collars. Altogether, the coat of arms, crest,
&c., are convincing evidence that Tom Brassey’s
first parent came over with the Conqueror, and
was one of the leading secrets of Hingland’s great-
ness.

“ A chariot which attracts attention in the town by its originality is that of Andy Barber.
The waggon is made of a dressed slab square box firmly fastened and bolted together, and mounted
on wheels, which are blocks cut off a round log. The team consists of a young bullock in the
shafts (which are made of saplings) and a horse in the lead. The turnout certainly did not cost
much, but it is more useful than some more showy conveyances and the owner carries all his
wheat, flour, chaff, and other products on it. In fact, it answers all the purposes of a waggon,
buggy, and cart ."—From Bligh (N.S.W.) Watchman.

The great difficulty in the way of reform-
ing the Judges’ relation system in N.S. Wales
lies undoubtedly in the case of Mr. Langer Owen.
Now, at any rate, Mr. Owen’s extensive practice
is but little dependent upon his relationship to the
chief Judge in E piity. Moreover, his practice con-
sists solely of Equity work and it is to that branch
of the law that he has devoted all his attention.
If, then, he were no longer allowed to practise
before Mr. Justice Owen, it is hard to see what be
could do or to vvhat court he could go. Here un-
doubted hardship would be worked. But in the
other cases the same difficulties do not exist in
anything approaching the same degree. And
something should be done before, through lapse of
time, interests become, in a manner, vested.

# • #
As an utterly squalid rom nice, the story
of Kate Norton, alleged to have been murdered
by Arthur Buck, in Melbourne, is almost unique.
The deid worn in was married at the age of 17,
and lived for a few years along with her husband
in a wooden cottage in a dingy Melb. suburb —one
of those cottages which is elevated on four posts
and has always a goat crawling underneath,
and a lame dog in the back yard, and the
gate tied up with string. Then she took
to drink, and the ill-assorted pair threw the
time-payment furniture and gimoracks at each
other, and finally separated. The husband “got
into trouble ” for illegally using a horse and
cart, and the wife continued drinking, and
lived for a while with a precocious youth with
a cigarette thumb; his name was Buck and his
age about 19. By-and-bye he went up
country ; and then she lived up an alley
with a laborer. The alley was a foul,
dog-infested place of ill-repute, the laborer
was an “unemployed,” and their chief means of
support was what the woman “ earned on
the streets.” She came home one night from
her miserable avocation with her throat cut from
ear to ear. The deed is said to have been done by
her former paramour, with a cheap German razor,
which he bought for 2->., said 2s. being raised by
pawning his vest with one Harris, in a back street
a t Richinon 1. Consider all the elements of that
romance : The girl-wife, the wooden cottage ele-
vated on four posts, the husband who “illegally
used a horse and cart, ” the paramour (aged 19)
tfibh the cigarette thumb, the alley, the uuem-
ployed laborer, the woman of the streets, the cheap
German razor, the vest that was pawned for 2s.
with Harris, and the final epitaph—“ Kate
Norton, who came home with her throat cut, aged
27.”

enoe in post-mortem examinations, and an exten-
sive acquaintance with feminine anatomy, is
Kate Norton aforesaid. btie was, says the
veteran doctor, “one of the loveliest women, in
res p ec t to figure, that I have ever set eyes upon.

Her face was a terrible oae the face if a
hardened Bacchante—but she had the limbs of
the Venus di Medici. I have seen many female
figures in my time, alive and dead? but I really
tliiiik that hers was the most perfect I ever saw.’’
And, as he said it, he sidled his head with the
sapient air of a wine-connoisseur who lias been
testing the bouquet of a choice vintage.

A skull was picked up on Sfc. Kilda (Molb.)
beach, the other day. The eyes remained in their
sockets, and there was a patch of scalp about the
size of half-a-crown adhering to the occiput—no-
thing more; and yet a poor woman turned up
almost immediately and claimed the skull as that
of her missing husband, and found five or six
witnesses who all positively identified the frag-
ment and condoled with the lone widow. Then

COONONG BILL: “Say, Mick , where shall I start on this Wanky concertina?”
the coroner went to work, and demonstrated
beyond doubt that the skull could not possibly
have belonged to the man in question. Not long
back a forsaken wife in another Melb. suburb laid
energetic claim to a derelict body, insisting that
it was the mortal remains of her beloved husband,
and was hardly convinced when “ the real
Mackay ’’ turned up—and was sued for his own
burial-expenses! Rev. Haweis, lecturing on “Is
Marriage a Failure ?” is mistaken in not dwelling
upon the eagerness of wives to identify missing
husbands amongst the miscellaneous human frag-
ments at the morgues.

Mrs. Mountrnorganaire D’Arcy has been
more than usually “indisposed ” of late, and has
been recruiting at Venice. Her eldest daughter
is shortly to be married to Mr. Hankey, while the
second girl, Violet, who is dark and foreign-look-
ing like her handsome mother, is in Paris culti-
vating what promises to be a good voice. Fortune-
hunters begin to regard the D’Arcy “millions”
more critically, realising that there must be many
bites at the cherry—what with one son a budding
diplomat, a second preparing for the army, a
third at Westminster school, and Miss Johnnie
D’Arcy not out of the nursery.

One of the funniest of the Austral Salon’s
medleys was that got up to give Pastor Haweis a
pat on the back. The little cleric did not come;
and consciousness that the arrangements failed
of their significance sat gloomily on the brows of
the promoters. Not even the knowledge that
they had “bagged Lady Holled Smith” could
alone for the absence of the Hamlet of the
occasion.

In these days of dwindling population and
vacant houses, Melbourne architects see little
light ahead. One of the brotherhood, well-known
as the tenor of a fashionable choir, now manfully
hawks milk in a fashionable suburb. Though
ai led in his new calling by the natural advantages
of pink cheeks, curling hair and a tuneful jodel,
the ex-architect fears that some dark winter morn-
ing he may bash in those clammy milk-cans and
go back to bed, regardless of consequences.
Another architect, who formerly represented
his firm in an important Victorian city, lately
went prospecting, resplendent in a scarlet shirt
belted with revolvers and picnic knives and forks.
At present, he earns ss. per week and tucker by
cooking for his party, and declares he is merely
waiting for the end. However, a camp-rule, for-
bidding any before-meal mention of the “ Gaiety
girls within hearing of the cook,” shows that
“there is life in the old dog yet.”

A Western timber-station ad-
vertises for a doctor. A splendid
chance for a young sawbones to
study smashes. The guards run
alongside the trams, ■ which go at
half-speed, and sometimes the big
logs slip off the trucks. Then-
well, the logs are rolling-pins, and
the men are dough until the bottom
of the gully is reached.

In a Melbourne club fre-
quented by city men there has of late
been much gambling by day and
night. The other day, as a result, it
is alleged, of a dispute at play, cul-
minating in “You’re a liar,” two
well-known men looked themselves
in a room and, fist to fist, fought it
out. One wore a diamond ring,
which cut his opponent fearfully,
but still it was not quite aone-sided
affair, and the pair had pretty well
battered each other beyond recog-
nition before the door was burst
open. They have since been formally
expelled from the club, which many
peaceable clubites are now leaving.

How they run church bazaars in
Maoriland. A (say) Matmgatauri lady
owns a very attractive oil-painting,
and the promoters of a recent bazaar
persuaded her to lend it to them
merely for display in one of the stalls.
The owner attended the bazaar and saw
her picture carefully hung, but the day
before closing noticed a label on it
“To be raffled.” She at once rushed
to the committee to ask what was
meant, and was told that it would
be all right. Not satisfied with this
assurance, she also consulted an ecclesi-
astical dignitary, who told her not to
worry a bit —she’d get her picture back
safe enough. So she kept quiet and put
her] trust in the Lord. Meanwhile, the
sale of tickets for the raffle went merrily
on. When the show closed the lady
received her picture back with a polite
note from Rev, Dash to the effect
that he had been fortunate enough to
win it and had much pleasure in re-
turning it. She took it and said
nothing, on the principle that a shut
mouth catches no flies.
m * *
A Chinese leper has escaped from
the lazar-house at Sorrento (Vic.), and
up to time of writing had not been
recaptured. All that is definitely
known is that he took the train at
Mornington, and got out somewhere
between there and Melbourne, and
then vanished into the infinite. No
one knows in which carriage the scaly
outcast travelled, or who sat next to
him, or whether he smote his infected
breast when he found himself enjoying
the sweet air of freedom and filled the
atmosphere with germs, or whether he
mashed a girl on the journey, or any-
thing else about him. All that is
known is that he is a Chinaman and
a leper, and that he is shedding germs
somewhere.
The safety of Toorak, the home of Mel-
bourne’s aristocracy, is watched over at night by
a single constable. He is enough. No sane bur-
glar would waste his valuable time in Toorak
nowadays.

city. It was a comparatively wicked and enjoy-

making the final turn for home. A photo, of Mr
Fisher is not procurable. Even his fellow V.R.C.
potentates couldn’t induce him to face a camera
along with the rest of them, though they brought
all their eloquence to bear some years ago. There-
fore a sketch of this good topical subject appears
on The Bulletin’s page of Melbourne illustra-
tions.
Large landed estates in England do not
always prove an unmixed blessing to their inheri-
tors. Considerable coin reserves are required to
maintain an establishment, as well as to keep the
lands in order, and there is generally an increasing
drain on the resources of the proprietor. The
Australian landowner goes to work in a different
way, however, and believes implicitly in the
general principle that money should be earned
before it is spent, and that interest should alway g
be earned on investments. The St. Alban’s estate,
near Geelong (Vic.), is a remarkable case in point.
The property cost .£108,300 ; the administration
since purchase has been liberal (some would say
lavish), and yet the investment has paid 5| per
cent, on its capital. This is the estate (Mr. W. R.
Wilson’s) which is to be distributed on the art-
union principle by “ Tattersall ” (Mr. George
Adams) at an early date. The views published
elsewhere represent a couple of pictures taken in
the garden, and will serve to convey a faint idea
of .the beauties of this famous property.

News has been received by the Californian
mail that such leading men of California as Alan
Gardner, Esq., M.D., of Santa Barbara; L. M.
D. Davies, Esq., M.D., Portland, Oregon ; J. J.
Gingles, Esq., M.D., Portland, Oregon; W. T.
Bell, Esq., M.D., San Francisco; P. Crowley,
Esq., Chief of Police, San Francisco ; have
unanimously given testimony of the toilet re-
quisite, “Amykos,” which, besides all its good
qualities, is also an excellent preparation against
all the bad influences to which we are always ex-
posed during the cold and moist winter months.

Sydney Fresh Food and Ice Co. has com-
pleted some big new contracts for regular shipments
of fish from Maoriland ports. One of the most
brilliant achievements of the F.F. &I. Co. to date
is the introduction to Australia of the M.L. blue-
cod, a fish superior to any other imported from
whatsoever quarter, and the demand for which is
now enormous.
Many ladies who favor that most becoming
fashion of tailor-made gowns have lost sight of the
King of Ladies’ Tailors in Australasia—Mr. B.
King, late of Collins-street, and, we are sure, will
be pleased to discover him again. We are able to
give the information. We now find him with
Messrs. Hicks, Atkinson and Sons, the successors
of the old-established firm, Moubray, Rowan and
Hicks, who, under his guidance, have made a
special department for Ladies’Tailoring—a branch
which it is not surprising to see daily increases in
favor. It is to Mr. King that ladies have to be
grateful for a new patent riding-skirt, which
minimises the danger of a fall.
