May 14, 1895.
My Dear Moorabinda—
The Sydney Town Hall ball, to provide funds
for the erection of a new operating-room at the
neatly-kept Lewisham hospital for women and
children, was very successful. You never have
any startling decorative novelties sprang upon
you at a big public ball, but the floor was fast, the
basement supper artistically decorated with
yellow ’mums, and the music lively, with one or
two novel effects. It certainly puts more cling-
someness into the common or garden waltz to
have the orchestra playing a few dreamy bars of
the Intermezzo from “ Cavalleria,” or other real
harmonies, before gliding into the commonplace
three-four beat; if this is going to be tire feature
of the coming ball-season a new joy will be added
to the Byronio dance. Dr. MacOarthy’s musicianly
“ Lyceum ” valse was one of the dance numbers,
also a waltz by Mr. G. Sykes (composer of the
clever “Canala” waltz) which I missed hearing.
Though some amateur compositions are a dis-
tinct addition to dance-music, yet I think the
wholesale system of scoring no matter whose
polka or waltz for full orchestra, and “ringing it
in ” on the public, is an Australian symptom that
needs watching. The success of a waltz depends
so much on the music that it is a heinous offence
for a conductor to substitute the “Wattle and
Dab” or “Damper and Mutton” for Strauss’
“Blue Danube”—unless the former happens to
have made a big local hit.
The weakest point about the Lewisham ball was
most certainly the dressing, taken as a whole. I
was not a bit impressed with the gowning of the
Acting-Governor’s girl-folks, and the invariably
well-grdomed Mrs. Edward Hutton didn’t come.
Exceptions to the general air of dowdiness were :
Mrs. Prendergast, in a rich black frock, with a
simple boutonniere (man’s size) on the corsage ;
Mrs. Maguire (one of the hon. secs.), in buttercup
brocade, with sleeves, and so on, of creamy chiffon
caught with violets; Mrs. W. Brennan, in helio-
trope silk and velvet; a pretty society pianiste,
in creamy silk, with a moire silk sash and plenty
of white chiffon ; and a very stylish unknown,
who sported an exceedingly pretty gown of flesh-
pink corded silk, with a waterfall back of flesh-
pink tulle—big pink sleeves off the shoulder, with
straps of brown fur and some brown-foliaged roses.
Several women wore off-the-shoulder sleeves (a
style that looks as if almost the very worst
we feared would happen when our old shoulder-
straps slipped had at last come to pass), but I don’t
think the uncomfortable pattern which Geraldine
Ollitfe introduced to Sydney when she played the
adventuress in the “Ideal Husband ” will have a
very large following. I may be miles out when
the numbers go up, but still I can’t help think-
ing that a fashion beloved of demi-mondaines (to
judge by the illustrated papers) will not be the
only wear. Even at this early stage it suggests
cigar- boxes. Heaps of men turned up at the
Lewisham ball,: headed by Acting-Governor Bar-
ley (who opened the ball with Mrs. Maguire) and
Admiral Bridge, and great cheerfulness marked
the proceedings in consequence. The committee
will clear nearly -£lOO as the result of their efforts.
I was somewhat amused at the ball-room method
of a society woman at the L.H. ball, who was the
direct opposite of the old-fashioned maiden who
used to be “led forth to dance” according to the
ballads of her day. This Sydneyite did her own
leading, thank you. It was really interesting to
see her dart forward to meet a cavalier and grab
him by his large white-kid-covered hand and tow
him to scratch—it seemed quite New Woman-y,
and the cavalier always looked so frightened that
he was quite en suite.
Man’s innate bashfulness is the worst handicap
the Emancipated Woman has to deal with. She
has not only to break down her own conventional
barriers, but there are man’s ages-old little foibles
and prejudices and notions to be reckoned with
besides; and when she has fought herself free of
her own skin and looks round for applause, lo and
behold ! man creeps further into his shell and
hopes to goodness she isn’t coming in his direction.
If men would only meet women half-way in these
matters, and not leave the liberty-seeking women
to do all the battling, things would no doubt be
made much easier.
But that will never be. Man is a hunting
animal, and only likes that which he catches him-
self. There is no poetry in game bought at the
poulterer’s.
The inter-Australian lawn-tennis tournay at
Sydney Cricket Ground was very entertaining on
Friday afternoon, when Saturday’s crowd was
missing, and even late-comers got a good idea of
what was going forward on the chalked-out
squares. A well-dressed audience looked on, and
received vagrant tennis-balls on its brand-new
parasol with charming equanimity. It looked a
very lady-like mob, too —sober winter gowning
has the same subduing effect on the color-loving
Australienne that mourning has on most people—
the quiet draperies lend an air of refinement quite
English, don’t you know. Here and there were
splashes of color—one lady hoisted a tartan gamp
which seemed an absolute invitation to “ come
under her plaidie.” The ever-juvenile Henry
Bracy, in autumn-leaf brown, accompanied by
Mrs. Bracy in black with rosella pink bows in her
toque, took such an open-mouthed interest in the
matches that one would think that W. and M.
meditate staging a lawn-tennis set.
The formidable array of brown boots visible
would have plunged a blacking-manufacturer into
the depths of despair. Wherever the naval con-
tingent turns out strongly there are always plenty
of tan feet; and the ostler-shaved mariner off duty,
in his loud checks and battered straw hat with his
coat-of-arrns on the band thereof, was well repre-
sented at the tenuis-jousts.
I was not overcome by the stage presence of
the players. I never did admire the lawn-tennis
girl. If she plays according to science you can’t
tell her head from her heels, and if she plays
“pretty’’ she poses too much and sacrifices the
game. The dress isn’t becoming, either—it is too
sjiort for grace, and then you get such to-be-con-
tinued views of the heelless tennis-shoe. Two of
the playeresses were little girls in blue, and the
neat, blue cambric dresses with black ties and
belts looked most becoming until the heat of the
encounter, when the fatal color which would give
a turnip a hectic appearance made the cerulean-
dad damsels look apoplectic. The two in white,
with pale-blue belts and ties, looked the neatest,
ti Ut t>V v ' ew Pedestal revelations I think that
the Bloomer baglet really ought to be adopted for
tennis if the girls came out boldly in pantaloons
we would at least know the worst at once.
The play was rattlingly good in one of the
ladies’ doubles, and I think the best she-racqueter
was a girl in white. I didn’t know anything
about her science or her pretty strokes, but I do
know that she more than once jumped at least 6ft.
in the air to intercept a wicked-looking ball. I
felt rather sorry for some of the players on
Friday. Two frantic youths, with their skins
working freely and gasping for breath, had about
six people looking on ; and two more in the dis-
tance, who also appeared to be leaving no stone
unturned, had an approving audience of one old
lady one players’ fond and faithful grand-
mama, I presume. The great mass of the audience
clings round the stars at the tennis tilt.
The Gov. Duff memorial-fund closed, the other
day, with the modest total of £B4 in the hat—
which, however, will doubtless prove sufficient for
the purpose of erecting a neat memorial tablet
in Fetteresso church. When you come to think
of it a small sum (only very small amounts were
asked for) is best for the memory of a ver3' plain-
living gentleman, and for the artistic reputation of
Australia. It is never safe to trust too much to
the pure taste of any committee, and if the Duff
memorial women had found themselves “rolling
in gold,” the chances are that something in mink-
veined marble, with gilt lettering, and set in gold,
like a set of shop-teeth, might have been the
awful result. There is nothing like forced
economy to ensure good taste in matters of this
kind.
Somebody rose in N.S.W. Assembly, the other
day, and demanded to know how much longer the
Governorship of New South Wales was goin’ to be
hawked about and refused by Imperial hangors-
on ? And Premier Reid, speaking in that subdued
tone of dignified secresy always used by magnates-
in-office when they are forced to allude to
the mysterious workings of the Imperial Authori-
ties—about which they know just as little as any-
one else—“ absolutely denied that the position
was being hawked about.” He had written to the
Agent-General about it, but the fact was that
there was a due sense of the importance of the
position, and the Imperial Government was
making every effort to secure the services of a
gentleman who would be acceptable both to the
’Ome hauthorities and to the people of the
province.
But Australia is getting very tired of this
“ mutually-acceptable ” story. Several recent ex-
amples prove that the LG. takes very little trouble
in making sure that the round peg doesn’t get into
the square hole, and the ways of Providence are
much less mysterious than ‘the gyrations of the
potentates who arrange who’s to govern which of
Australia. A very recent example was a case in
point, and I wonder if South Australia, threatened
with Saint Buxton, is quite ready to subscribe to
the mutually-acceptabie theory ?
The third assemblage of Sydney Home-readers
occurred last Saturday afternoon at Sydney Uni-
versity (absolutely the most unreachable place of
entertainment in Australia for the majority of
people), and was attended by a crowd, principally
feminine, who diligently “did” the programme,
from receptions to refreshments—with stress on
refreshments. They stormed the School of Mines
theatre, where Prof. David gave a deeply scientific
lecture on “The Ice Age of Australia” (fortu-
nately combined with lantern-slides—a naked
savage would grasp a lecture on bi-metallism if
you threw in a few gaudy illustrations to help it
out), so nobody came away exactly in the dark..
After that, there was tea in the cold and dignified
Great Hall, weakly decorated (the hall) with
frivolous potted plants, giving the effect of a
Sphinx with a paper collar on; and before that
there had been a Shakespearian recital by Mr.
Harry Leston, in a wonderful frock-coat.
The dressing was extremely “literary.” I
noticed several buttons hanging by a single thread,,
and one woman’s pocket was half unsewn. Like-
wise, there were several ladies present whose hair
had apparently been chewed by rats. And whatr
do you think of a facially-comely Home Readeress;
in cinnamon-color, a corkscrew fit, with big green
silk sleeves, and a green plush Tara o’ Shanter
very much on one side of a touzled head of yellow
hair? She evidently wanted a course of fashion-
plates for her mental pabulum. One H.R. in
snuff-color had two goose-quills stuck in her hat in
a style reminiscent of the “King of the Mos-
quitos,” and I was amused, too, by a professor in
pale-grey, who had a giddy little Fritellini hop in
his walk that must be most trying to the risibili-
ties of his classes.
I don’t quite know whether I approve of the
Home Readers or not; anyhow, before giving
my final verdict, I would like to penetrate into
a H.R. manage at the trying moment when
the aforesaid H.R, is cooking breakfast. If
she had a frying-pan in one hand and a book (pro-
vided it wasn’t a cookery-book) in the other, I
would be con ; but if she controlled her itch for
literature until her duty was done (as they
say in the “ Gondoliers ”) then I would be most
distinctly pro.
By the way, the Home Readers have a
little grievance against themselves, so I hear.
It is stated that the circles (each suburb has its
circle) are getting too deep. There is a depressing
tendency to disembowel only the most obscure
works for the edification of the leading lights of
the seance, and the meetings, as a consequence,
sometimes consist of two or three spectacled and
bony ladies engaging in an animated discussion on
prehistoric primordial protoplasm, while the rest
of the gang sit about yawning and fidgeting until
time’s up. It is contended by people who have
the interests of the A.H.R.S. truly at heart that
the ambitious effort to get up a bright and shining
circle may wreck the society, which numbers all
sorts of intellects from “brilliant lunatics to
blithering idiots,” as Miss Olliffe says in “An
Ideal Husband,” in its serried ranks. As it is
going now, dull individuals (who, after all, are the
people most in need of this sort of intellectual
goad) drop out of some of the circles from sheer
inability to understand what on earth the learned
members are talking about.
“ La Fille de Madame Angot ” was the Saturday
quick change at Sydney Lyceum for the Comic
Opera people, who must sometimes feel they dunuo
where they are in such a stormy torrent of revi-
vals. lam pleased to record that tenor Tapley,
who made a most intellectual Ange Pitou (in pale-
grey legs, with a trotting-mare’s rosette on his
hat), had to resort to the prompter in the second
act. The letter-perfect Mr. T., who has always
answered to his cue through a long and shifting
season, was becoming almost superhumanly pre-
cise, and his sudden fall from grace seemed to me
a very reassuring circumstance. As Larivaudiere,
Mr. Brownlow had a good millinery part (though
the vocal chances were nil), being most admired
in a certain claw-hammer coat of red and gold
brocade and a pair of miraculously-fitting white
kid tights, which made you wonder how on earth
he got into them. By the way, in the disguised
scene (sloppy calico clothes, a shock of ginger hair
and a stoop), the gods professed to recognise
Frederic Villiers, and loudly hailed him as the
war-correspondent.
The dressing of the ladies was wildly fanciful,
and went to prove that petticoats were not worn
in the days of Madame Angot—only a skirt split
up to the belt on one side, revealing a long
and, as a rule, shapely nether support. Nellie
Stewart, in a short white satin bridal-dress, and
poke bonnet all trimmed with orange blossoms,
had a flesh-pink rounded limb trussed with white
ribbons; and Florence Young, in an Empire
gown of black-figured lace over pink, thickly edged
with pink roses and lined with pink silk, had also
a pale-pink leg (this split skirt idea does give
rather a one-leggy effect), very trickily embroidered
in black butterflies as far as the eye could reach
which was quite a long way, too. In the last act,
Nellie wore a peppermint-striped skirt and a
crimson velveteen bodice, in which she looked
bewitching ; and Florence Young wore a mag-
pie blend. Madame Soldene was immense in
any and every sense as Amaranthe, and acted a
small part like an artist. But what a failure Rose-
year made of Pomponnet, who has the prettiest song
in the piece ! By the way, one noticed in Madame
Angot that Nellie S. doesn’t allow any stage-
lover to fold her to his manly chest just because
he is her accepted Johnny in the stage-directions.
It was very evident on Saturday that “ principals
only ” is her motto when it comes to the hugger-
mugger part of it; the unknown swain is charily
allowed to imprint a warm) embrace on her elbow,
or some such frozen frontier.
From Nicholson and Co. I have received a
parcel of songs that are mainly a joy, with one or
two reservations. “Where Shadows are Not,”
by Frederick Covven, is a soberly-sweet song, well
suited to a mellow mezzo-soprano, and quite free
from sentimental exaggerations. On the merits
of Mascagni’s “Preghiera” it is unnecessary to
dilate, as its reputation is already made ; but it is,
perhaps, news to know that it can now be obtained
in keys to suit all voices, from heavenly soprano
to bull-dog bass —which rather looks as if we were
in danger of an overdose of “Preghiera.”
“Shadowland,” by Stephen Adams (sung by
Patey), is a very dainty song in gavotte time, to be
had in all settings, but is best heard in contralto.
Another song best in the low pitch is “ Do ye hear
thechildren weeping, ’’Mrs. Browning’s tragic words
set to droning, but effective, music by Beprend; but
“ Dapple Grey,” by the same composer, is see-saw
and silly—the kind of lilt people who can’t sing
invariably do sing. “ Home Dreams,” by E. St.
Quentin, is also rather mawkish ; but “ Duncan,”
by Lawrence Kellie (sung by Belle Cole), is a
very promising song for a good emotional con-
tralto to storm the platform with.
From that up-to-date publisher of the latest
local novelties—H. J. Samuells— I have received a
■copy of G. B. Allen’s “ Softly fell the Sunshine,”
which Madame Esty sang so tellingly in Sydney
Town Hall, and I am just as pleased with it on
nearer view. It is a mezzo-soprano song, and
words and music are very quaint and pathetic.
Lady Holled-Smith, Melbourne’s new social
light, is evidently a most considerate hostess. I
hear that at her late At Home women with com-
plexions like the inside of a shoe or the back of a
doormat noted with satisfaction that the light was
shaded and shuttered out of the reception-rooms,
and only a faint twilight gloom pervaded the
scene.
The Misses Fitz-Stubbs, of Sydney, assisted bv
Miss Violet Aaron, of Melb., have opened a dress-
making and designing establishment in Regent-
street, London.
Yours affectionately,
SAPPHO SMITH.
SPORTING NOTIONS.
A totalisator argument. At S.A.J.O. Autumn
Meeting, May 4, Bombastic was beaten by a head for first
place in the Hurdles. £1786 was the total sum in the
machine, and three persons only backed Bombastic. Had
he won there would have been a div. of £550. In the old
days, what a scoop for the bookies 1 In this instance the
tote laid 550 to 1, the books laid 15 to 1. The totaiisator
must come.
• * • *
Australian racing clubs using totalisators are
increasing stakes and profits, the public at the same time
obtaining fair odds. The bookmaker thrives where stakes
and profits are being extinguished in his interest, and the
public receive prices in no way a reflex of the odds against
them.
• • • *
S.A. bookmakers are continually in trouble.
S.A.J.O. recently made a by-law against tote-commission
agents, aimed, of course, at the Hebrews, but so sweeping
that it is now illegal for one man to put a sov. in the tote
for another, such being declared unlawful by the club.
The layers have formed a strong union and intend to fight
the clubs in the court.
• • * »
The first day’s results at the S.A.J.C. meeting
were indeed calculated to produce a horrible massacre of
bookmakers in every region where Ikey influence keeps
the Tote at bay. The Adelaide machine naid divs. as fol-
lows over six events: £56, £U 75., £35 145,, £9 155.,
£6 ss. (only four starters), and £ll 7s. Thirteen started
for the race in which First Bolt paid a £56 divvy, and two
of them were much ranker outsiders than the winner. It
must be a very infatuated punter who can look at those
figures and refuse to admit that punting with bookmakers
(in Australia) is ruinous folly. “Any price outsiders ! ”
shouts Ikey when 13 numbers are up. “ What price First
Bolt? ” say you. “Height to one!—not good enough?
Well, ten to one to you, hy G—d ! ” shrieks the reckless
Hebrew. For the next month you plume yourself on
having pulled off a good bet, although you merely got
one-fifth of its proper value. The difference between a
bookmaker’s “outsider” and an outsider as it appears in
the Tote’s returns is always ridiculous.
♦ • * »
Melb. has more racecourses (shearing-sheds)
than any town five times its size. It has one for every
day in the week (Sundays included) and three over. In a
word, there are 10 racecourses within a few miles of Melb.
* * • •
Had Sheet Anchor lived, it would have been a
fight between him and Trenton for pride of place at one
stud. In his short career, the Anchor left his mark in
The Harvester, Caspian, Duhallow, Our Jack, and a dozer
other first-class gallopers.
* » ♦ *
Nearly every Sheet Anchor horse raced in Melb.
at present is coming to the front. The “defunct’s”
latest successes were two wins in one day at Aspendale
Park (Vic.). Tiny took the half-mile handicap, and Gun-
boat the welter. Tiny is from Streamlet, a good mare, and
Gunboat is full brother to Best Bower. Sheet Anchor
looks like taking a position not many removes from
champion sire, shortly.
* * * *
Elswick’s win at S.A.J.O. meeting on the
second day broke his long accumulated turf ice. On this
occasion, the Burrumbeet people backed the son of Nor-
denfeldt up to the hilt, and he won—on the post by a
head.
• » * *
Tiie bad luck of Mrs. Carr’s filly, Monastery, in
just losing Tattersall’s Cup, the S.A.J.C. St. Leger, and
Adelaide Cup, by a bare head, reminds one of Devon’s
last year Spring performances in Victoria, when he ran
second in the Caulfield, Melbourne, and WilKamstown
Cups.
»** ■ *
J. Anwin, who rode the Adelaide Cup winner,
Elswick, won the ’9l Melb. Cup on Malvolio. He was
almost unknown at the time, but having ridden the son
of Malua in his work was allowed the mount in the race
and won.
* ♦ » *
The V.R.C. has decided to try the experiment of
addingfouroccasional Saturday meetings to its annual pro-
gramme-one each in August, September, April, and June.
The September meeting will inaugurate the season which,
in the booming days, was known as the Racing Carnival,
and it ought to catch the ghost of popular excitement on
the hop. Saturdays in August and April should pay
expenses, but the June fixture looks less promising. Any-
way, it has been good enough for the rival V.A.T.C. to
hold meetings in every month of the year, so the senior
club, with its greater means of entertainment, can hardly
do wrong by arranging four new bills.
* * ♦ *
See how the Devil whispers in a man’s ear !
“ Willie ” Cooper, of Sydney, lately sold his colt Kirk-
connel, and lo and behold 1 it straightway goes and wins
the Two Thousand Guineas and becomes first favorite for
the Derby. Now the more or less gilded William will go
and fish for another Kirkconnel, and won’t find one in 20
years.
• ♦ * •
Who is “Ophir, care of Mr. Harold Benson,
Grand Arcade, Queen-street, Brisbane, Queensland?”
“Ophir” has on hand “a 100,000 sweep at ss. ; highest
prize, £BOOO less 10 per cent. Every subscriber to get a
prtze.” The Bulletin is becoming interested.
* ♦ » •
It would be as well if each proprietary course
had its own handioapper. It has often happened thai a
handicapper has taken an unreasonable view, to put it
mildly, of the abilities of some particular owner’s horses;
hut, with a system of separate handicappers, an owner
under the ban of one handioapper would have a show of
getting fair play at the other courses.
* * * *
Rosebery Park might well give trotting a trial,
ruling owners from the start with a rod of iron. In the
past, the proprietors of the courses have been as much to
blame as the owners for the “ stiff ” running. Had Can-
terbury and Kensington in the “past” “senl-up” the
crooked ones without fear or favor, the trotting-horse
would now be in a fair way to take the place in our
weekly sport which he so well deserves.
* * * *
Scarpia, formerly the property of S. G. Cook
(Vic.), is now located in W.A. That he is considered the
local champion sprinter is shown by the fact that he is
never allowed into any handicap under lOst. He won at
York recently with 10st. 31bs. in the saddle, and in the
approaching W.A.T.O. races he is called upon to carry the
steadier of lOst. 91b.
♦ • * •
Dear Bulletin, —A correspondent asked the
height of the biggest Australian thoroughbred. I think
Mr. S. Miller’s Mernder can undoubtedly claim the honor.
This giant stood 17.2, and was the cleverest fencer of his
time. His great races with Mr. Pearson’s madbrained
mare, Lady Ashton, will be remembered by racegoers.—Z.
* * * *
Dear Bulletin,—A live “ dead-bird ” : A swal-
low’ flew into the office (at Adelaide), and the door having
been closed the bird was asked for a straight tip on the
list for the Goodwood Cup. When Mostyn was called the
swallow immediately settled on the clock ; the list being
re-read, when the name of Mostyn was mentioned, down
went the bird on the clock again. At a casual mention
of Mostyn the swallow once more settled on the clock.
On Mostyn being mentioned for the fourth time the
swallow at once re-perched on the clock. This gained
him his release, and three clerks straightway subscribed
3s. 4d. each to back the dead-bird of a live one—and same
evening divided £l7 16s. result of tote investment. True
bill.—Loco.-Liar, Holy City, Crowstralia.

Carbine has been insured in England against all
risks for £13,000 at 9 per cent.
♦ ♦ • *
There is quite a mania just now in England,
writes Tub Bulletin’s London correspondent, for insur-
ances of all kinds in connection with racing. The under-
writers at Lloyds will take any risk so long as it is well
paid for. They must have dropped a good bit over the
insurance of racing - fixtures postponements, so many
meetings have been abandoned. The moving spirit in
this new form of gambling is Mr. Henry Nixon, deputy
chairman of Lloyds, who is a rather hoi sey man. He was
in Australia buying remounts for India with old Col.
Bobbins, and rode two or three races at Homebush on
Whisker, a well-known horse in those days. Nixon
married a daughter of the late Canon Allwood. One of
those who underwrite these risks is Mr. Hawke, who is
the moving spirit in connection with the anti-gambling
league. This is inconsistency if you like, but principles
go to the wall.when the question of £ s. d. comes in.
HARDPHIZ, the centre forward, getting into
form for the approaching season.
The interprovincial lawn-tennis tournament
closed last Saturday. The day was flue, and Gregory s
courts were excellent, but at 11 o clock, when the first
contest began, enough of the night’s dew remained to
make the ball grow heavy. Green had gone home to
Victoria, so Crossman, of Armidale, challenged VVebb. the
holder, for the single championship of N.S.VV. Webb, the
domdity player in this five-set contest, had been out of
form the whole week past, and Crossman very often
scored by playing steadily and waiting for Webb s mis-
take. Still Crossman has condition, agility and skill, and
Webb, even at his best, would find him a most difficult
opporent. As it was, Grossman outclassed Webb by
winning straight off the first three sets and the coveted
championship.
Tile match which delighted the hearts of the
zealots was the ladies' single championship between Miss
Shaw of Victoria, and Miss Dransfleld, of NS. W. Miss
Shaiv is a brown-eyed, symmetrical girl with a plump,
muscular figure. She understands that tan shoes
make a woman look a near relative of Crusoe s
savage, so she wears black ones. Miss Dransfleld is of
slivhte'r build, and wore a broad leather band around her
These girls gave, perhaps, the finest exhibition of
ladies’ tennis ever seen in Australia, and the way they
contested splendid rallies, and the resolution with which
they flew in and out and around the court and took im-
possible balls, caused the male onlooker to pray that both
had had three months’ training. Otherwise they will nob
be able to get up for a week.
Miss Shaw, being the stronger, served with more
force and returned harder. She also excels Miss Drans-
fieid in placing the ball, and this skill of hers won her
many a game. There is one tiling Miss Dransfleld has-
an excellent nerve, which enabled her to win the second
set after losing the first. The girls played at their best in
the rubber, but Miss Dransfleld was tiring, and when Miss
Sha!w had won five games to one the spectators thought
all was over. Then Miss Dransfleld’s nerve helped her to
win two games ; but the severe rallies exhausted her and
Miss Shaw won the next game and the match. After a
few minutes both ladies came out again and Miss Shaw
partnered Golquhoun and Miss Dransfleld paitnered
Webb for the Ladies’ and Gentlemen s Challenge Pairs.
Golquhoun played with such brilliancy that he seemed
to take the spoils of victory under his arm from the very
first, and he and Miss Shaw won ajery one-sided contest.
George Giffen, in a Pall Mall Gazette article,
considers that the best Australian Eleven would be
slightly stronger than Stoddarfs Eleven. Possibly A
Stoddartian travelling eleven have to be taken as they
come into the field, some of them a trifle “ off’ in health,
others suffering from liquor, perhaps ; whereas Giff. a idea
of a “ representative ” Australian Eleven is a thoroughly
sound and sober body of men selected at the last minute
on their latest “ form.” Judging from what one knows
about the Englishmen’s vagaries in Australia, one would
an'v that they at their best all round, were slightly
superior to the best eleven procurable by Giffen. But
they didn’t “blow” on the subject. Stoddart and 00.,
from first to last, flattered their opponents in preference
to flattering themselves.

A Masterton (M.L.) senior football club has
unanimously adopted the following sensible rule:
“Should any member be disabled through injuries
received on the field, a levy of 6d per week shall be
struck on all the club members, which shall be handed to
the injured member upon production of medical certifi-
cate,” &o. . , *
The name of Beach is not likely to soon fade
from the annals of Australian sport. Arnold Beach, son
of “ the people’s William,” is entered for a coming con-
test at Clyde River regatta. The youngster is spoken of
as a shapely, likely customer. #
Americans begin to discover a peculiarity about
Australian fioxersf noted by Tim Bulletin a few years
ago. Though Foley was for his weight a punishing hitter,
his best pupils, such as Griflo, Jem Hall and Steve Q Don-
nell, are remarkable for the quickness rather than the
force of their blows. Even the matchless Peter Jackson
owes his position, not to hard slogging, but to the grand
science which enableshim to stall off opponents until they
get worn-out in their vain efforts to hurt him. The
reason is that these men throw their weight forward.
Their blows have a less distance to travel, but they cannot
get up steam as a man does whose centre of gravity is
farther back. However, their quickness enables them to
forestall and slop a hard hitter. Hence Foley’s rule
“ Let the beggar beat himself.”

After Essendon’s first match for the Vic. foot-
ball premiership, in which the new goal-kickers turned
out awful failures, the committee telegraphed to Thur-
good, their champion goal-kicker of last year, who is pro-
specting in W. A., to come over at once. Of course Thur-
good is not a profes-nonal. He is not paid wages—he
only gets “expenses.”

Melbourne senior football clubs gradually settle
into their premiership positions. At present Melbourne,
Essendon, Fitzroy, and Geelong are the only unbeaten
clubs.

Alsop, of Richmond (Vic.) F.C., seems this
season’s likely “Thnrgood.” He kicked four beautiful

» ♦ * *
At Hawke’s Bay races (M.L.) one of the leading
side-shows was a budding Maori girl in knickerbockers.
'Twas a magnificent fit, and she walked like a soldier.
After the meeting she mounted a bike, and pedalled to
town ; none of your namby-pamby pedalling, but good
lean-over man’s style. In the evening she appeared at
the theatre, and created quite an impression, as her
costume was unchanged.

The two Sids., Gregory and Walford, were the
only players in the Sydney premiership cricket-matches
during the past season to compile two individual scores of
a century and over. Gregory’s were, 152 and £lOO ;
Walford’s, 134 and 114.

Horses are a drug in the market in the North.
A smart auctioneer had to sell a cart-horse, and received
a bid of 15s. “ Fifteen shillings !” he bawled. “Who
says ‘ a sov. ?’” “ Does the harness go with the horse ? ”
queried the bidder. “No—only the halter!” “Then—
-1 withdraw my bid.”

Heffernan, the Maorilander, and Kelly, of
Natal, fought last month in the Amphitheatre, Johannes-
burg. £9OO were taken at the doors and the excitement
was intense, but the affair proved very tame and easy for
Heffernan. Kelly went out in the fifth round. Sullivan
has challenged the victor, and they will meet in May.

A seaman named Gillespie has got a year s
gaol from the Sydney naval court-martial for
striking his superior, petty-officer Buckley. At
last is solved a great problem : it is now quite
clear who struck Buckley.
The submarine mine-failure at the recent
military operations at Sydney Heads was, it is
explained by General Hutton, in a letter to Mr.
C. E. Stokes, due to the use of Obsolete apparatus,
and in no way reflected on the efficiency of
robnrite as an explosive. The sale of roburite,
despite the naturally conservative bent of miners
in such matters, is rapidly increasing in Austra-
lasia.
| The Bulletin hears that an old woman,
nearly-related to one of the four banged Mount
Bennie beys, has written to ex-Governpr Caring-
ton cordially congratulating him on the birth of a
son-and-heir. The congratulations in question
were of the order which might reasonably be ex-
pected from an old Irish party whose kinsman had
been convicted and slaughtered (after an all-day-
and-all-night sitting) by a sleeping jury, on the
evidence of an utterer of forged cheques and an
arsonist.
“ Crom-a-boo,” the irresistibly funny and
rollicking Irish song of the Hoben Brothers, which
Sappho Smith opined would prove “a terrible
success,” is already proving. Lonnen has taken
it up in London, and Lauri is to sing it in
Australia; it is to be well introduced in America,
and in Maoriland. where that clever comedian,
Percy, of the Pollard Lilliputians, is making a
feature of it. It is also a universal success and
heard everywhere. Paling’s have already cleared
the first big edition, and have got out two others
in E flat and E. It is certainly the funniest Insn
football-song ever written, and the press every-
where has risen to describe it as the best comic
song of the day, and funnier than “Killaloe ’ or
“ Ballyhooley.” It,is just the thing lor an aspir-
ing oomique, pro. or amateur."
A remarkable fact. Mr. W. Joshua
Wilson, aged 58, was terribly injured in the Red-
fern railway-accident of 21st Oct. last. Mr.
Wilson’s ease, in fact, was regarded as one of the
most serious of all, he having completely lost one
eve and been otherwise permanently injured in the
smash. At one time while he was in the hospital
his life was despaired of. Yet, instead of prose-
cuting his undoubted claim to heavy damages, he
presented himself the other day at the office of the
NSW Railway Commissioners, and presented
I to’ the ’department a voluntary release from all
claims on account of the injuries he had sustained.
The Bulletin never before heard of such a thing.

Again the Chow. Warwick (Q.) district
has yielded for the past year 300 tons of tobacco
to 1000 acres of land. Most of this has been grown
by Chinese, who produce five tons to the Euro-
peans’ one.
Preliminaries of a debate between Miss
Bad ham and Mrs. Ida Halloran, two of Sydney’s
most intellectual women, are being talked over.
The ladies differ cardinally on many important
topics.
Popular Australian superstitions :—Snakes
don’t die till sunset.—lt is always the night-shift
that strikes the reef with rich patches in it.—A
horse with three white feet can't win a race.—
Cattle on a camp see ghosts.—To find a corpse in
the bush is a sign of violent death for the finder.
On 10th July, 1788, Lieut.-Gov. Major
Ross wrote to Mr. Under-Secretary Nepean :
“I will in confidence venture to assure you that
this country will never answer to settle in, for
though I think corn will grow here, it cannot be
in less time than 100 years hence. I therefore
think it will be cheaper to feed convicts on turtles
and venison at the London Tower than be at the
expense of sending men here.”

“ Higinbotham’s oratory was Eclipse first,
and the rest nowhere. Malice was its essence, a
Robespierrian hate at a white heat. He had a
lovely, innocent, boyish, almost childish look,
and would get up in the Legislative Assembly,
amid the thronged green leather benches, stam-
mering out in a low tone, after the most hypo-
critical oratorio fashion, well knowing that, in a
few minutes, he would have Langton and all that
crew—in a Higinbothamic sense—lashed to frenzy,
the House surging about like the sea-waves, while
as the reporters affectionately named him,
was churning out slow, deliberate, staccato sen-
tences about England as a foreign power, and the
man Rogers at the Colonial Office, who was the
real Czar of all the British colonies.”— Herbert
Palmer.
The only known cure for influenza is Cole-
mane and Sons Eucalypte Extract.
Louis Ellis, who is retired as Yic. sheriff
after 43 years’ service, is a Sydney native. His
brother is Angel Ellis, who has been time out of
mind accountant to the Metropolitan Gas Co.
Mr. Louis Ellis left Sydney in the forties as a
mere boy before the gold-digging days, to seek his
fortune in Melbourne. When under 20 years of
ago he had worked himself up into partnership in
a clothing store at the Bourke and Elizabeth-st,
corner, facing the Post-office. From that he
graduated to Harris’s big clothing store, now occu-
pied by the London Tavern in Elizabeth-street.
If Louis Ellis, the retired sheriff, should now
write his “ reminiscences,” as ’bis the fashion to
do. he will have much to tell of the curious and
notorious criminals who have, so to speak, passed
through his hands. As also of the different hang
men who, had they failed to do their office, would
have caused Ellis to take that last duty of a
sheriff upon himself.
For rheumatism use Oolemaue and Sons
Eucalypte Special Oil.

THE HISTORY OF A SOAR.
jj l6 ka°k my right hand —just about the
middle of it there is a small scar, half as big as
a threepenny piece perhaps. You would never
notice it unless I showed it to you, and even then
you would have to look sharp to see it. But it’s
there, all the same, and will be until the hand is
returned to dust. It dates back forty years, that
scar does. Going home from school one day
another boy and I quarrelled and fought. Strange
to say, it wasn t about a girl either. Anyway he
drew his jack-knife and stabbed me in the back of
the right hand. The wound, I remember, was
8 jTu’ 0 was sore an< i inflamed for months,
and hardly a day passed but something hit it, or I
knocked it against something, and so made it
worse. My whole available body appeared to be
concentrated in that sore. You know how such
tilings are. They are like boils, and if there is any
comfortable spot to have a boil there are loads of
money waiting for the fellow who discovers it.
Lately we have received two letters, both con-
taining an identical expression, namely, this •
Everything seemed a trouble to me.” Now, none
of us are so tough as to be proof against trouble
but when everything is a trouble to a man the very
heart inside of him must be tender and touchy
And, mercy knows, it is so often enough. Grief
will do it, worry will do it, and illness too.
One of these letters, or, at least, the writer of it
goes on to say:—“lt was in March, 1880, that I
felt tired and languid, and without my usual
energy. Before that I had always been strong
and active. Now I was low-spirited and melan-
choly ; everything seemed a trouble to me. At
first I had a bad taste in the mouth, a poor
appetite, and all I ate gave me great pain in the
chest and sides. Even fish and poultry gave me
as much distress as more solid food. I was
constantly spitting up a sour, acid fluid which
caused a miserable feeling in ray throat and
mouth.
“After I had been for some time in this con-
dition I was attacked with gout in my hands and
feet, which confined me to the house now and
again for a week or two at a time. The parts
became inflamed and swollen, and gave me excru-
ciating agony. I could not bear anything to touch
them. For four years I was subject to these
attacks. The doctor who attended me was able
to ease me temporarily, but I was soon as bad as
ever.
I n _ the middle of April, 1888, I read that cases
like mine had been cured by Mother Seigel’s Cura-
tive Syrup, and I thought I would try it. I hadn’t
taken more than half a bottle when I felt relieved
This encouraged me to keep on with the
and in a comparatively short time all symptoms of
the disease left me. Since then I have enjoyed
excellent health, and whenever I feel a twinge of
my old enemy a dose or two of Mother Seigel
soon sets me right.—Yours truly, (Signed) M.
Leahy, 49 Grosvenor Terrace, Grosvenor Park
Camberwell, London, September 22nd, 1892.’’ '
So much for the facts as Mr. Leahy felt them.
What has science to offer in explanation of them ?
This. Gout, rheumatism, and biliousness are three
complaints arising indirectly from an overworked
liver, or, more properly, from indigestion and
dyspepsia. The poisons so engendered may lie
hidden and unfelt for a long time, and then be
suddenly rendered active by mental worry,
exposure, over eating, wrong eating, or any of a
dozen other causes. The kidneys fail (following
the stomach and liver), the acid poison remains in
tiie blood and sets up inflammation in the joints,
and the retained fluids produce dropsy. All sorts
of disturbances are apt to go with this condition
of things, every one less a disease in itself than a
symptom of the one cause indigestion and
dyspepsia. The heart and lungs areoften attacked
in sympathy. Cure the torpid digestion, and an
all round improvement at once succeeds. To do
this is in the power of Mother Seigel’s Curative
Syrup—as, perhaps, in the power of nothing else.
It was most unfortunate that this gentleman
suffered for eight years; and no wonder, mean-
while, that his mind was sensitive as his body to
every approach. Knowing what he now knows he
feels safe. There is no darkness but ignorance, and
the German Nurse shows “The way out.”
London, October, 1892.
J.M.P.

A self-denial week for Melb. Hospital
commences on 20th inst. The Agr and Argus
have come out with strong leaders on the matter,
but without any tangible proposal. Thb Bulle-
tin therefore proposes that every Victorian should
for that week deny himself his morning paper and
send the pennies to the fund.
The Oollingwood (Melbourne) tragedy—
the axe-splintering of a suspected wife and mother-
in-law—is a meteoric attestation of the distinctive
sex-fashions in murder; and is appropriate just
now when the “Dean poisoning case ’’ is seething
unquietly in the public mind. Poisoning is the
chosen method of murder by the average female.
Bloody violence, with firearms, knives, axes, or
bludgeons—something with brains and gouts of
gore in it—is the sort of slaughter that the
mind of the male assassin broods over ; with suicide
thrown in for dessert, as in the Oollingwood case.
Hearne’s Medicines, as advertised in
another part of this paper, form an imposing body
of salves and lotions and pills and draughts. Mr.
Hearne goes on the principle that you shouldn’t
fire a charge of duokshob at the Tantanoola tiger,
or sight your artillery for Botany when the Rus-
sians are at Bondi; but should, as it were, fit the
punishment to the crime, and the remedy to the
ailment. So he has fixed up 61 different kinds of
cures for as many kinds of diseases, and a man
who has No. 38 disease is fixed off at once with
No, 38 cure, while a man who has a complication
of No. 38 and No. 11 can mix his medicines accord-
ing to directions and get there just the same.

—THE—
Central Queensland
Gold-mining Proprietary.
With, say, 1300 aqres of Freehold and Leasehold.
Consolidating several proved lines of Quartz

Addenda to Reports in Prospectus already issued
(for private circulation only).
Addenda to Reports,
When the notorious fallibility of the average
mining expert was borne in mind, it became a
matter ot some difficulty to secure a perfectly
independent report. In view, however, of obtain-
ing large monetary assistance from England, the
Irustees took the precautionary course of solicit-
ing the aid of the Chamber of Commerce, Q.,
who, in the interests of the district, might under-
take an inspection of the property. This was
done, and the following documentary evidence
(abbreviated) speaks for itself. It is estimated
that 12 grains to the load will pay.
Declaration Duly Sworn.
We, Joseph Hickson, George Hickson, and
Ldwm Hillary Rolls, of Gladstone, in the Colony
of Queensland, do solemnly and sincerely declare
that we visited the property known as Hew Zea-
land Gully Freehold on the ninth and tenth days
of March, 1895, and that we dispensed with the
services of the mining manager in charge of the
property. [Here occur details of the inspection. )
We have no interest whatever in the above
property, directly or indirectly, and we are of
opinion that the entire Gully systematically sluiced
on a face will average at least six pennyweights of
gold to the load of (say) 120 dishes averaging 22 to
241 b. to each dish. This declaration is made oi
our own free will, and is our joint and several
unbiassed opinion and belief.
Signed
f JOSEPH HICKSON.
\ GEORGE HICKSON.
(.EDWIN H. ROLLS.
Note.— The inspection was made under the
supervision of the Hon. Sec. Chamber of Com-
merce, Q.
_ , „ Gladstone, April 22nd, 1895.
To the Trustees,—
I have much pleasure in forwarding you here-
with a Declaration of Messrs. Joseph Hickson,
George Hickson and Edwin Hillary Rolls, all of
Gladstone, in the colony of Queesland.
2. These gentlemen are well-known to me as
very straightforward, reliable, honest men, who
are now living a retired country life, having done
well in the mining ventures of ‘this district. They
are experienced miners of many years standing.
. 3. Ihe Report sworn to by these gentlemen is,
in my opinion and to the best of my belief, a most
reliable one. The Boyne-Calliope Freehold is
well favored here as a valuable mining property,
and if worked by hydraulic sluicing should far
exceed the estimate of six dwts. to the load, as
stated in their report.
4. I would add that the locality referred to is
, renowned for a phenomenal output of alluvial gold
i in the early period of its discovery, and it is my
firm belief that the scheme of hydraulic sluicing
you propose adopting should reveal other import-
ant auriferous deposits.
„ 0. C. BRESLIN,
.President Chamber of Commerce and Mayor of
Gladstone.
Witness: D. M. JONES, Gold Warden Port
Curtis District.
Extracts from a letter to the Trustees from
JMr, Jason Holes > 1/. /*., Representative for
Port Curtis in the Legislative Assembly of
Queensland :
... „ April 23rd, 1895.
You will allow me to express my opinion on the
Boyne-Calliope Freehold, which includes New
Zealand Gully and two miles of dredging area on
the Boyne River. The locale of this property
' includes the richest deposit of gold that has yet
been discovered in the district of Port Curtis. . .
Hampered owing to the Government in its early
innocence permitting the property to become free-
hold. (Thus excluding the miners),
i So far as I glean, the scheme of hydraulic
sluicing is exactly what is wanted, and should
result in a rich profit to the shareholders. lam a
resident of the district for over forty years, and
have always taken a deep interest in mining.
Wishing you every success in your undertaking,
which I feel certain will exceed the most sanguine
anticipations,
Faithfully yours, JASON BOLES, M.L.A.
Bank Certificate of the Gold Already Taken
from Netv Zealand Gully.
The Australian Joint Stock Bank,
The Trustees, 19th March, 1895.
Boyne-Calliope Freehold Syndicate.
Gentlemen,
I have much pleasure in certifying—so far as
banking and other authentic records will permit—
to the yield of gold, obtained during the first rush,
from New Zealand Gully, the locale of which is
nearly the centre of the present Boyne-Calliope Free-
hold. The Australian Joint Stock Bank has let n
established at Gladstone (originally known as Porn
Curtis) for thirty-one years, and possesses inter-
esting information and valuable records of the
local goldfields.
From the records to hand, and the estimates of
those now in the district who shared in the rich
harvest, the gold obtained during that period with
cradle and pan was upwards of 280,0000zs
valued at about ,£3 17s. 6d. per 0z-
£1,085,600. I was assisted in this estimate by
three respected residents who took part in the
rush, and describe the gold as “ literally taken out
in tons.”
Experienced miners are positive that the go’d
has come from rich reefs in the neighborhood of
New Zealand Gully, and their opinion is shared
by the Government Geologists.
The scheme of Hydraulic Sluicing you propose
adopting will doubtless be the means of bringing
these sought-for reefs and other important auri-
ferous deposits to light.
Further information and copies of the private
prospectus may be obtained from the Brokers to
the Proprietary, Messrs. SOTJTAR & HOARB.
P.O. Chambers, Pitt-st,, Sydney; O. VAN
ASSCHE, Esq re., Queen-st., Melbourne; or
from the Legal Managers, Messrs. SMITH &
BARKER, 373 Collins-st., Melbourne.

SKIN DISEASE
CUBED BY
Ayer’s Sarsaparilla
Mr. Geo. W. Burtner, Keezletown, Va.,
writes, as follows:
“ Shortly after leaving college, I was troubled
with a skin disease which showed itself, first, at
the ankles. Physicians pronounced it eczema,
and treated me for that complaint. The erup-
tion crept slowly up my limbs, and on the body,
until it enveloped the whole frame. It gave me
infinite trouble with constant itching, casting off
of dry scales, and a watery liquid which would
exude from under the scales. I treated it for
over three years unsuccessfully, and was unable
to check it, until I began using Ayer’s Sarsapa-
rilla. I used three bottles of this medicine and
was completely cured —my skin becoming as
smooth and clear as before.”

A SONG FOR THE NIGHT.
O the Night, the Night, the solemn Night,
When Earth is bound with her silent zone,
And the spangled sky seems a temple wide,
Where the star-tribes kneel at the Godhead’s
throne ;
O the Night, the Night, the wizard Night,
When the garish reign of day is o’er,
And the myriad barks of the dream-elves come
In a brightsome fleet from Slumber’s shore I
O the Night for me,
When blithe and free,
Go the zephyr-hounds Ob their airy chase;
When the moon is high
In the dewy sky,
And the air is sweet as a bride’s embrace!
O the Night, the Night, the charming Night !
From the fountain-side in the myrtle shade,
All softly creep on the slumbrous air
The waking notes of the serenade ;
While bright eyes shine ’mid the lattice-vines,
And white arms droop o’er the sculptured sills,
And accents fall to the knights below,
Like the babblings soft of mountain rills.
Love in their eyes,
Love in their sighs,
Love in the heave of each lily-bright bosom;
In words so clear,
Lest the listening ear
And the waiting heart may lose them.
O the silent Night, when the student dreams
Of kneeling crowds round a sage’s tomb;
And the mother’s eyes o’er the cradle rain
Tears for her baby’s fading bloom ;
O the peaceful Night, when stilled and o’er
Is the charger’s tramp on the battle plain,
And the bugle’s sound and the sabre’s flash,
While the moon looks sad over neaps of slain ;
And tears bespeak
On the iron cheek
Of the sentinel lonely pacing,
Thoughts which roll
Through his fearless soul,
Day’s sterner mood replacing.
O the sacred Night, when memory comes
With an aspect mild and sweet to me,
But her tones are sad as a ballad air
In childhood heard on a nurse’s knee ;
And round her throng fair forms long fled,
With brows of snow and hair of gold,
And eyes with the light of summer skies,
Aqd lips that speak of the days of old.
Wide is your flight,
O spirits of Night,
By strath, and stream, and grove,
But most in the gloom
Of the Poet’s room
Ye choose, fair ones, to rove.
D. H. DENIEHY.
AMANS AMARE.
A cottage small be mine, with porch
Enwreathed with ivy green,
And brightsome flowers with dew-filled bells,
’Mid brown old wattles seen.
And one to wait at shut of eve,
With eyes as fountain clear,
And braided hair, and simple dress.
My homeward step to hear.
On summer eves to sing old songs.
And talk o’er early vows,
While stars look down like angels’ eyes
Amid the leafy boughs.
When Spring flowers peep from flossy cells,
And bright-winged parrots call,
In forest paths be ours to rove
Till purple evenings fall.
The curtains closed, by taper clear
To read some page divine,
On winter nights, tlm hearth beside,
Her soft, warm hand in mine.
And so to glide through busy life,
Like some small brook alone
That winds its way ’mid grassy knolls,
Its music all its own.
D. H. DENIEHY.
D. H. DENIEHY
[The above charming and little-known verses appeared
in a Sydney magazine in 1817, when Deuieliy T was a lad
of 19.]

Rev. Haweis, discussing “Music and
Morals,” says “ the English National Anthem is
nothing but John Bull shouldering his way
through a crowd, knocking everybody out of his
road,” whilst “the frivolity and fickleness of the
French nation is exemplified in the Marseillaise.”
Ye gods ! there are force, and vigor, and fighting
in the dreadful, tailor-made “God Save the
Queen,” and only mincing femininity in “The
Marseillaise,” bred of revolution, and breathing
the fierce aspirations of a whole people battling for
freedom !
Dear Bulletin, —Was Dr. B. W. Richard-
son’s book on “Drink,” which is used as a text-book
in Maoriland State schools, really written by the
alleged author? It is full of-blunders in elemen-
tary chemistry and physiology. And it is ridi-
culous from a literary point of view. He gives a
quotation from Pythagoras (p. 150), whereas we
have nothing left of Pythagoras’ writings. He
calls the last stage of drunkenness (p. 104) “com-
plete concatenation of circumstances.” He con
fuses the arteries with the capillaries (p. 97), and
says the latter have muscular coats. He say-
(p. 76) that water consists of two parts hydrogen
and one part oxygen. This would terribly confuse a
teacher, and is absolutely wrong, because you can see
by the next page (in which bespeaks of carbon) that
he means, if he means anything, parts by weight.
The writer of pp. 75 to 79 evidently did not know
what a chemical formula such as CH4O (methyl
alcohol) meant. And, generally speaking, the
book is quite unfit for school use. The whole
drink question turns on the simple fact that
alcohol does indisputedly cause crime. Whether
it is a food or not is quite beside the question, for
it is so expensive that nobody would seriously use
it for that purpose. What you want to tell
children is that good potable spirit can be made
for about 2s. per gallon, and when we pay 6d. a
nip we not only pay 15s. a gallon to the Govern-
ment but the same or more to the publican, and
often get the spirit impure. They, when they get
older, must judge what legislation would minimise
the present evils.— W. F. Howlett, Makaretu,
M.L.

Land of the Crocodile .
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
By the centrifugal scattering forces at work
in early colonisation, many of the people most
interesting historically are projected to the re-
motest fringe of settlement. Hence we find in
Queensland, even far west and north, some of
those who form connecting Jinks between the pre-
sent and the early years of the parent province.
Yesterday, I met an old lady who, in her maiden
days, as Miss Williams, was lady’s-companion to
Miss Burke, and dressed her on the morning of
her marriage with E. Deas-Thomson. Mrs.
Thomson, by the way, was credited with writing
all her husband’s best speeches.
* # #
Here is an authentic snake story, reveal-
ing remarkable presence of mind. A Miss
M’Ewen, now Mrs. Ellis, lived with her mother
and sisters on the Brisbane River in a locality
notorious for black snakes. The girls were so
accustomed to these reptiles that the sudden
presence of one inspired no fear whatever. The
dairy was the great attraction for the snakes, as
they are very fond of milk. Family seated at
dinner. Kate M’Ewen called to the servant to
bring her a basin of milk. “The jug of milk is on
the table,” said her mother. “Bring me a basin
of milk and be quick about it,” said Kate, with-
out moving a muscle. The basin was handed to
her and she quietly placed it on the floor close
to her foot. Then a black snake slowly un-
coiled himself from the calf of her leg and
glided over to the milk. This is one of several
instances of Miss M'Ewen’s astonishing self-
possession. What would an ordinary man have
done in similar circumstances ?
* * #
.When our Crown Ministers were out on a
recent grand tour, to “ make ourselves acquainted,
Mr. Speaker, with all the wants of this great
country,’’ one of them, in a moment of deplorable
weakness unworthy of a modern statesman’s
dignity, asked a brother Minister, “Are we
fully justified in the expense of this little festivity
at the present time ?” To which the other, “ That
implies an abstruse problem in political ethics
which life is not long enough to solve ! Charley !
bring in another half-dozen of champagne! ”
Which recalls (I have just been reading American
papers) the two niggers in the fowl-house. “ Ain’t
it werry wrong to do this sort of thing?” said the
conscience-stricken partner. “ Dat involves a
great moral question which we ain’t got no time to
discuss. Hand down that big white rooster!”
9 * m
The champion humorist among Australian
explorers was M‘Douall Stuart. "Referring to
hoisting the Union Jack on Central Mt. Stuart,
he says : “ We gave three cheers for the flag, the
emblem of civil and religious liberty, and may it
be a sign to the natives that the dawn of liberty,
civilisation and Christianity is about to break
upon them ! ” Only a Scotchman would indulge
in such reckless jocularity in the centre of a con-
tinent from the whole of whose living aboriginal
inhabitants, and the countless indignant ghosts
of those who were dead, there was even
then ascending to Heaven one deep, well-
founded curse on the day when the “emblem of
civil and religious liberty,” and those who carried
it, came in sight of the Australian coast!
• * *
A venerable pioneer, with a phenomenally
clear memory, was recently telling me that he was
a youth of 16 at Bathurst in 1826, when Holloway
kept the “ Bathurst Classical and Mercantile
School,” and the “Bathurst Hunt” Club rode
forth in green jackets turned up with velvet, a
dingo in gold on the collar, and gilt buttons with
“Bathurst Hunt” on them. He still owns two
of the buttons. The men with the clearest
memories are those who never learned to read. A
woman’s memory is usually more reliable than a
man’s.
* * *
An episode in the life of a Brisbane
society woman, who, after a long interregnum,
had a quite unexpected piccaninny. By way of
novelty she was seated on the bedroom floor wash-
ing the infant in a large washhand-basin. It was
her very first attempt, and she was nervous. The
plump little living “ roley-poley ” slipped out of
her hands and vanished in the soapsuds. Mamma
thereupon went promptly off into hysterics, and
when some woman heard the awful uproar and
rushed promptly into the room, she found the
infant sprawling on the floor in a pool of soapsuds
and mamba lying on her back beside the empty
basin, which she had fortunately kicked over in
the first excitement.
* 9 •
You lately printed 0. R. “Daly” instead
of “Haly,” late P.M. at Dalby, and one of the
squatting pioneers of Queensland, Charles Robt.
Haly and R. R. Mackenzie, our fifth Premier,
were returned for the Burnett to the first Parlia-
ment, in 1860. Haly had a station on the Logan
in 1845, and Dr. Lang stayed there, on his way
over from the Clarence, via Mt. Lindsay. Old
Haly was one of the whitest men in Queensland.
He had to bear much chaff in the House for his
advocacy of the natural grasses, especially the wild
carrot, and the use of salt. A wiser generation
may have good reason, and the necessary sense, to
remember old Haly’s prophecy.

ownership?” said a pompous J.P. to a bush wit-
ness in a N.Q. police-court. “ Only the gripes a
couple o’times,” was the reply. This fits in with
the evidence of a Dutch witness who, when asked
if his pig had no ear-marks, observed that “Ze
only ear-marks he haf vas two curls mit his tail 1”

The train from Perth to Southern Cross
“ waters up ”at Northam. There has to be taken
on board not only enough water for the upward
journey to the Cross, but also sufficient for return.
So it often happens there is attached to the train
a truck of water to every truck of luggage carried.
“Everyone will find in the Scriptures
what he wants to find,” says the Rev. Dr. Strong,
of Melbourne. “It is the broad spirit of morality
and religion for which we should look to the
Bible.” The clergy might recommend Zola’s
history of the Rougon family in this spirit.
“Everyone will find in the Scriptures what he
wants ” —including a living.
At Cue (W.A.), the other day, a prisoner
escaped from the watchhouse in the absence of
the town’s solitary policeman—whose three com-
rades were down with typhoid. The prisoner
managed to get 22 miles through the desert from
the watchhouse, but was eventually recaptured by
black trackers, who managed to “scent the trail,”
although the fugitive had taken off his boots and
tied to his feet sacks, in which he walked.
It is a grievous matter, moans R.C. Bishop
Doyle, that the Irish tongue should be neglected,
“seeing it is so much more perfect a language than
English.” The same silly claim has been made for
half-a-dozen other tongues by those who forget
that language is a tool, and that the most “per-
fect ” tool is the one that works best. Philological
symmetry is but an accident of language—practi-
cal utility is the surviving essential; which is
why, despite protesting shrieks about theoretical
perfection, it is always English that “goes !”
A very old man, who said he was
nearly 94 years of age, canvassed Bast Mel-
bourne the other Sunday seeking assist-
ance. He said if he could get along for a little
while he would be all right, as he had been pro-
mised one of the cottages at the Old Colonists’
Home, and the present occupant couldn’t last
long, as he was “very old!” The ancient pro-
duced undoubtedly genuine testimonials to show
that he had been 21 years in one situation, and 16
in another, but his savings had dwindled away.
For coughs, colds you can’t beat Colemane
and Sons Eucalypte Extract.
There is at least one man in Melbourne—
a journalist, by the way—who now wouldn’t pay
sixpence for a peep at the most irnnroper ballet on
earth. The thought of a SultanVharem in gauze
robes de nuit has no charms for him. At one fell
swoop ho has exhausted all the possibilities of
spicy spectacle, and his contempt for Living Pic-
tures is profound. A few weeks back the jour-
nalist in question was waiting about the marine
premises connected with a suburban ladies’ swim-
ming-bath, when a pale-faced woman rushed
forth from the inner sanctum and implored him to
come and save a drowning friend. There was no
time to waste in considering one’s reputation as a
modest married man. A human life was at stake,
and a strange female was tugging at his coat-tails,
so the journalist made his way to the bathing
reserve, where 200 women, some of them screaming
in the water and the others shrieking from the
platform, directed his attention to their sister in
distress. He plunged into the ocean and, being
an excellent swimmer, rescued the insensible
bather without much difficulty. The poor woman
had merely fainted, and a little brandy and mas-
sage soon restored her to consciousness. Then the
journalist became likewise conscious of what was
happening around him, and appreciated for the
first time the fact that he was revelling in an
unusual experience. The two hundred bathers
were now all out of the water, and clustering
round the rescue party. Those who had been
towelling themselves when the alarm started, had
forgotten to begin to dress, whilst many of the
remaining mermaids had broken loose from their
bathing apparel in the excitement of the moment,
and were gathered, wet and trembling, in the
background. The curious feature of the case was
their general indifference to the presence of a man
in their midst. True, he had all his clothes on,
and sticking to him. Perhaps the ladies thought
that his decent appearance leavened the whole
lump. But he didn’t. Like the right-minded
journalist that he was, the gallant hero gave one
hurried glance at the glittering array of flesh
and went off home to get dry.

EAGLE STREET, BRISBANE. Q.
Concerning a Dog-fight.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Dog-fighting as a sport is not much in
vogue nowadays. To -begin with, it iS illegal.
Nob that that matters much, for Sunday drinking
is also illegal, yet flourishes exceedingly. But
dog-fighting is one of the cruel sports which the
united sense of the community has decided to pub
down with all the force of public opinion. Never-
theless, a certain amount of dog-fighting is still
carried on around Sydney, and very neatly and
scientifically carried on, too principally by
gentlemen who follow the occupation of slaugh-
terers, and who live out Botany way and do not
care for public opinion.
* « •
The grey dawn was just breaking over
Botany when we got to the meeting-place. It was
Sunday morning, and all the respectable, non-
dog-fighting population of that stinking suburb
were sleeping the heavy, Sunday-morning sleep.
Away to the East the stars were paling at the
first faint flush of the coming dawn, and over the
sandhills came the boom of the breakers. An
intense stillness was over everything, and the
white-walled cottages of Botany were shrouded in
a faint mist. Some few people, however, were
astir. In the dim light hurried pedestrians might
be seen plodding their way over the heavy road
towards the sandhills. Now and then a van,
laden with about 10 or 11 of “the talent,” and
drawn by a horse that cost 15s. at auction, rolled
softly along in the same direction. These were
dog-fighters who had got “the office,” and knew
exactly where the chewing-match was to take
place.
» # #
The “meet” was on a main road, about
half-a-mile from town, and here some 200 people
had assembled, and hung up their horses and
vehicles to the fence without the slightest conceal-
ment. They said the police would not interfere
with them, and, in truth, they did not seem a
nice crowd to interfere with. One dog was on
the ground when we arrived. He had come out
in a hansom cab with his trainer, and was a white
bull-terrier, weighing about 40 pounds, “ trained
to the hour,” with the muscles standing out all
over him. He waited in the cab, and licked his
trainer’s face at intervals, to reassure that indi-
vidual of his protection and support. The rest of
the time he glowered out of the cab and eyed the
public scornfully. He knew as well as any human
being that there was sport afoot, and he looked
about eagerly and wickedly to see what he could
get his teeth into. Then a messenger came run-
ning up to the cab and demanded to know, with
a variety of expletives, whether they meant to sib
in the cab till the police came : also, he said that
the other dog had arrived and all was ready. The
trainer and dog got out of the cab, and we
followed through a fence and over a rise, and
there, about 200 yards from the main road, was a
neatly-pitched enclosure like a prize-ring — i.e., a
30-foot-square enclosure formed with stakes and
ropes. About a hundred people were at the ring-
side, and in the far corner, in the arms of his
trainer, was the other brindle.
« # *
It was wonderful to see the two dogs when
they caught sight of each other. The white dog
came up to the ring straining at his leash, nearly
dragging his trainer off his feet in his efforts to
get at the enemy. At intervals he emitted a
hoarse roar of challenge and defiance. The
brindled dog never uttered a sound. He fixed his
eyes on his adversary with a look of intense
hunger, of absolute yearning for combat. He
never for an instant shifted his unwinking gaze.
He seemed like an animal who saw the hopes of
years about to be realised. With painful earnest-
ness he watched every detail of the other dog’s
toilet; and while the white dog was making fierce
efforts to get at him, he stood Napoleonic, grand
in his courage, waiting for the fray.
• • •
All details were carefully attended to, and
all rules strictly observed. Most people think a
dog-fight is a go-as-you-please outbreak of lawless-
ness, but there are rules and regulations—simple,
but effective. Possibly one could even buy a book
containing the rules of dog-fighting. There were
two umpires, a referee, a timekeeper, and two
seconds for each dog. The stakes were said to be
JHO a-side. After some talk, the dogs were carried
to the centre of the ring by their seconds and pub
on the ground. Like a flash of lightning they
dashed at each other, and the fight began. Nearly
everyone has seen dogs fight—“it is their nature
to,” as Dr. Watts puts it. But an ordinary worry
between (say) a retriever and a collie, terminating
as soon as one or other gets his ear bitten, gives a
very faint idea of a real dog-fight. These bull-
terriers are the gladiators of the canine race. Bred
and trained to fight, carefully exercised and dieted
for weeks beforehand, they come to the fray exult-
ing in their strength and each determined to win.
E toh is trained to fight for certain holds, a grip
of the ear or the back of the neck being of very
slight importance. The foot is a favorite hold ;
the throat is, of course, fashionable—if they
can get it. These dogs sparred and wrestled
and gripped and threw each other fighting
grimly, and disdaining to utter a sound
under the most severe punishment. Their
seconds dodged round them unceasingly, giv-
ing them encouragement and advice. “That’s
the style, Boxer—fight for his foot.” “Draw
your foot back, old man,” and so on. Now
and again one dog got a grip of the other’s
foot and chewed savagely, and the specta-
tors danced with excitement. The moment
the dogs released hold of each other they
were snatched up by their seconds and carried to
their corners, and a minute’s time was allowed, in
which their mouths were washed out and a cloth
rubbed over their bodies.
# * #
Then came the ceremony of “ coming to
scratch.” After the first round, on time being
called, the brindled dog was let loose in his own
corner of the ring, and he was required by the
rules bo go across the ring (some 30ft.) of his own
free will and attack the other dog. If he failed
to do this he would lose the fight. The white
dog, meanwhile, was held in his corner waiting
the attack. After the next round it was the
white dog’s turn to make the attack, and so on
alternately. It, therefore, became evident that
the animals need not fight a moment longer than
they chose, as either dog could abandon the fight
by failing to go across the ring and attack his
enemy. While thefr condition lasted they used to
dash across the ring at full run ; but, after a
while, when the punishment got severe and
their “fitness” began to fail, it became a very
exciting question whether or not a dog would
“ come to scratch.” The brindled dog’s condi-
tion was nob so good as the other’s, and he
used to lie on his stomach between the rounds to
rest himself, and it several times looked as if he
would not cross the ring when his turn came. But
as soon as time was called he would start to his
feet and come limping slowly across glaring
steadily at the other dog; then, as he gob nearer, he
would quicken his pace and at last make a savage
rush, and in a moment they would be locked in
combat. So they battled on for 56 minutes till
the white dog (who was apparently having all the
best of it), on being called on to cross the ring,
only went half-way across and stood there growl-
ing savagely till a minute had elapsed, and so he
lost the fight.
» m *
No doubt it was a brutal exhibition.
Bub it was nob cruel to the animals in the
same sense that pigeon-shooting or hare-hunt-
ing is cruel. The dogs are born-fighters, anxious
and eager to fight, desiring nothing better. What-
ever limited intelligence they have is all directed
to this one consuming passion. They could stop
when they liked, but anyone looking on
could see that they gloried in the combat.
Fighting is like breath to them—they must
have it. Nature has implanted in all animals
a fighting instinct for the weeding out of the
physically unfit, and these dogs have an extra
share of that fighting instinct. Of course, now
that the world is going to be so good, and we are
all to be teetotal and only fight in debating
societies, and the women are to wear the breeches,
these nasty, savage animals are out of date, and
we will not be allowed to have anything more
quarrelsome than a poodle about a house—though
even poodles will fight like demons when they feel
like it. And the gamecock and the steeplechase-
horse and all animals with sporting or fighting
instincts must be done away with. Guineapigs
will, perhaps, be safe to keep, though even they
have a go-in at one another occasionally. And
the man of the future, the New Man, whose fight-
ing instincts are not quite bred out of him, will,
perhaps, be found at grey dawn of a Sunday morn-
ing with a crowd of other unregenerates in some
backyard frantically cheering on two determined
buck-guineapigs to mortal combat.

telling fortunes from cards, but passes by those
who do the same thing from hand-writing or
photographs, or from inspecting the palms of
hands.

ship,” “ Sport Worship,” &c., but not “Parson
Worship.” The latter vice, however, is nob
popular.
There are eight duly-qualified homoeo-
pathic doctors in Sydney, and one officially-
registered chemist. He is doing a large and
remunerative business. Homoeopathy gains ground
in Sydney. The Homoeopathic World, the cult’s
monthly journal, steadily increases the list of its
subscribers. One of the most constant and ardent
of its writers is a Sydney resident.
Ed. Bulletin,—-About the bullet from the
N.S.W. policeman’s rifle which “ travelled half-a-
mile without encountering any obstacle, and then
killed a woman,” Gordon, in his Khartoum jour-
nal, tells a more remarkable story. An Egyptian
officer was killed while lying asleep bn his bed-
stead by a bullet that could not have copie from
anywhere nearer than 4000 yards off. That is the
danger of giving policemen firearms for use in
crowded streets. If the shot misses what it is
aimed at, there is no telling what it will hit.—R.
Dear Bulletin, —“ Why are bulls more
savage in England than Australia?” The English
beast has had a reputation thrust upon him and is
assisted to live up to it. He has for generations
been a comic “property,” and occupies the same
place in the stock of the British bludgeon-humorist
as the coon Johnsing, with the chicken or melon,
does in that of his Yankee congener. Also, every
writer of commonplace country stories has worked
in that fearsome bull, and made him the medium
by which the stalwart young man with the
London Journal legs displays heroic presence of
mind and ousts the bold bad baronet from the
affections of the distressed damsel. The conse-
quence is that when & town young person
of either sex happens across a beast in the
country that is nob a calf and doesn’t quite
seem a cow, they give a wild shriek and
stampede for the nearest fence. The same
result would happen with Australian cattle if
people in a strange dress indulged in the same
procedure. Further —bulls, like other beasts, are
much as you bake them. In Australia man is lord
of the beasts—or thinks so, which is much the
same. In England nine-tenths of the people, un-
accustomed to animals, wouldn’t stay to argue the
point. Possibly, also, the sad Australian bush
may have the same depressing effect on bulls as on
bush poets. For the rest the Australian bull has
an out-door life, natural feed, little restraint, and
a fair amount of female society. The English
animal is cramped for space, ramps with good
living, wears a ring in his nose, has his amours
regulated by a man in hob-nailed boots with a
clay pipe, and—has his reputation to consider. —
Treble B.
